HEADLINE: Commercial Property/Westchester; 
A Grand View, and Metro-North Too

BYLINE:  By JOHN HOLUSHA 

DATELINE: IRVINGTON, N.Y. 

 BODY:
THE view from the Hudson River waterside here is expansive, with the Palisades visible across the broad stretch of water and the scene bracketed by the Tappan Zee and George Washington bridges.
 The developers of Bridge Street Properties are counting on the view and the prospect of modern office space adjacent to a major commuter railroad to lure tenants to a speculative 25,000-square-foot office building they plan to start constructing next month. 
 The building, whose facade will be 70 percent windows to allow occupants to enjoy the views and to admit as much natural light as possible, is the latest undertaking in a development that has transformed a large part of an old factory complex to offices and retail space.
It is also part of an effort by private interests and municipalities along the Hudson, stretching from Yonkers to Troy, to reclaim waterfronts long blocked by obsolete industrial plants that once relied on water transportation to bring in raw materials and ship out finished goods.
The manufacturing operations have mostly ceased or moved elsewhere, leaving decaying buildings, often with contaminated land, between the communities and their waterfront. The current trend is to clear away the problems and use waterfronts as an attraction, said Thomas Wright, executive vice president of the Regional Plan Association. 
"Water and rail transportation is the reason places developed in the first place," he said. "Over the last 10 to 15 years there has been a trend of putting amenities on waterfronts that will tie them into the regional economy."
The nine-acre Bridge Street site, which is west of Metro-North's Hudson River rail line, is the kind of place that could not happen if it were not already in existence. In 1890, according to the developers, a local company called Lord & Burnham acquired the right to fill in the river to create new land for its expanding business, something that would not be permitted today. The factory complex, which produced large greenhouses and boilers to heat them in winter, was built from 1910 and 1913, said Andrew Lyons, one of the co-owners of Bridge Street Properties. In those days the river was a major transportation artery, with riverboats carrying bricks from numerous brickyards in the area and stone from nearby quarries to build apartment buildings and brownstone houses in New York. River traffic has decreased since, although fuel oil and other commodities are still shipped upriver by barge.
Manufacturing at the site ceased in 1985 (Burnham Steel Boilers still operates in Lancaster, Pa.) and by the early 1990's the buildings were being used as a warehouse for a moving and storage company.
After joining his father's moving company in the mid-1980's, William Thompson decided that the complex of buildings could be better used as a waterfront business community. The development of parks to the north and south of the site by the village of Irvington added to its attractiveness.
THE partners first leased and then bought the 200,000-square-feet buildings and began converting them to office space. Large windows were cut into previously featureless brick walls and in some places floors were raised so that office workers would have river views while seated at their desks. But the industrial exterior of the buildings was preserved, as was much of the maple flooring and the cypress columns and ceiling planks. New glass was installed in the sawtooth skylights to bring in additional natural light.
Inside the old factory walls, new heating, air-conditioning and power and telecommunications equipment were installed to meet modern business requirements.
The complex is now home to about 50 businesses employing about 550 people and includes the Eileen Fisher women's clothing design company, the developers say. About a dozen of the tenants relocated from New York City, they add, and close to 100 workers reverse-commute from the city in another example of how good public transportation can promote development.
The rail link also allows companies who left the city to stay in touch with clients who remained behind. Executives can take uncrowded daytime trips into the city, hold meetings and depart before the crush of commuters starts heading home, Mr. Thompson said. 
Mr. Lyons said that "it is a lot different here than in the city, and the access to good transportation gives people the opportunity for a new lifestyle." That new lifestyle includes practices that would be unheard of in Manhattan.
"We like dogs," Mr. Thompson said. "We must have half a dozen people who bring their dogs to work occasionally."
The office complex also is close to the center of Irvington, a town of about 6,000 whose Main Street runs down to the train station. "It's a close-knit kind of place," Mr. Thompson said. "My daughter walks down here to my office once a week or so to do her homework." Mr. Thompson and Mr. Lyons also own a small boat club at the north end of the property.
In addition to the new three-story building, which is expected to be completed by the end of the year, the partners are continuing to renovate parts of the old factory. Combined, the two projects are expected to add 75,000 square feet of office space at the site this year.
One inhibitor to waterfront development, said Mr. Wright of the Regional Plan Association, was what he called "well-intentioned environmental laws" that require unrealistically pristine cleanups and leave uncertain the legal liability of developers and their lenders.
These so-called brownfield issues were not much of a problem at the site, despite its manufacturing history, Mr. Lyons said. Old fuel tanks are a common source of pollution as they rust and leak over time. But here the tanks were above ground and protected in vaults, he said
The design intention for the new building is that it appear to be the offices of the factory complex, Mr. Thompson said. "It needs to look as if it was designed in 1900," he said, "not a century later."
The new building is being designed to green standards, he said, which means maximizing energy efficiency and using recycled materials. Because so much of the facade will be enclosed by glass windows (with old-style sashes that can be opened) the glass chosen is one that admits light but reflects solar heat rays. In addition, the core of the building that contains the utilities has been compacted in the center, so that daylight can reach all the working spaces of the structure. The architect for the project is Walter Sedovic of Irvington.
The developers are exploring recycling some of the cypress planking taken out of the old buildings into wood blocks that could be used as flooring in the new building or in the continuing renovation of the old ones. But they have been forced to order old-looking new brick for the outer walls of the new building. "We tried to use recycled brick, but there was just not enough of it from the renovation operation," Mr. Lyons said.
Mr. Thompson said he expected to sign either a single tenant for the entire new building or tenants for each of the three floors before the building was completed. He said prime candidates were financial industry companies interested in following concerns like Morgan Stanley and New York Life that have recently established operations in Westchester Country.
"The floor sizes, at 8,000 square feet, are well suited to small mutual funds and financial consulting companies," Mr. Thompson said. "We hope it will be attractive to companies whose executives already live in Westchester."

GRAPHIC: Photos: Andrew Lyons, left, and William Thompson at site of their new building on the Irvington riverfront. (Top: Susan Stava for The New York Times; above: Bill Dawson)

HEADLINE: L.I. @ WORK; 
Nassau Links Housing and Economic Growth

BYLINE:  By WARREN STRUGATCH 

 BODY:
WHEN Raffaela Petrasek was hired as a vice president of the New York City Economic Development Corporation nearly 10 years ago, 42nd Street at Times Square was a seedy, down-at-the-heels place. 
 Last month, when she left to become the director of Nassau County's newly formed Economic Development Corporation, the seediness had largely given way to office towers and new hotels, renovated theaters and glistening new stores and tourist attractions. And as the senior real estate negotiator for the city and state, Ms. Petrasek gets a fair share of the credit for the turnaround from her former boss, Jonathan Fair, an executive vice president of the New York development group. Last month Peter T. Sylver, Nassau's deputy county executive, recruited Ms. Petrasek, a Huntington resident, for the county's own fledgling economic development corporation. Once again her mission will be to spur economic growth by nurturing strategic real estate deals. 
 But this time there is a major difference. The Times Square renaissance was built on adding heavily subsidized commercial buildings, but the county's plan hinges on encouraging the construction of 700 units of affordable housing. . 
Specifically, her job will be to spur economic growth by expanding the stock of affordable housing in Nassau County. It is an idea, several people involved in planning the strategy said, based on no existing economic development models, certainly none implemented on Long Island, but one that addresses one of the biggest roadblocks to economic growth. 
Mr. Sylver said the county hoped to recruit and retain businesses -- and revitalize downtown areas -- by deploying $7 million in federal housing money to create the 700 units. They will be new or renovated, he said, and would be built in commercial and downtown areas, perhaps even in industrial parks. 
The details, Mr. Sylver said, are still being worked out and he could not estimate how many of the units would be owned or with rented, or what sort of buildings would be built or where. 
He did say, however, that about 125 units would be earmarked for low-income residents and that the buildings would be scattered around the county, which was organized into 35 economic zones for this purpose last year by Thomas R. Suozzi, the county executive.
Mr. Sylver said the county was looking at a wide range of building types, including condominiums, town houses, stand-alone single-family structures, multiunit rentals and converted apartment houses with a mix of owner-occupied units sold at market rates and subsidized units rented to low-income tenants.
The county also plans to seek additional financing for the project from the federal Department of Housing and Urban Development.
"HUD housing is extremely important to our county, because the median income is so high," Mr. Sylver said. "A family of four can make $66,400 and still be eligible. So we're talking about teachers and postal workers."
Complaints about the high cost of housing on Long Island abound, and much of the complaining comes from business leaders. The Long Island Association, the area's largest business group, and the Long Island Regional Planning Board, along with many other groups, say that the cost of housing is one of the biggest deterrents to economic growth on the Island.
But complaining about housing prices has not yet translated into widespread support for government-financed housing initiatives. Lee E. Koppelman, the executive director of the regional planning board, said that most of the support for affordable housing on Long Island was limited to housing for older residents. "And seniors aren't part of the work force," Dr. Koppelman said.
The theory behind Nassau County's strategy is apparently untested. Mr. Sylver said that combining affordable housing and economic growth makes sense, even if this is the first time the two have been explicitly linked. But he added that how to capitalize on the benefits is still being sorted out by an advisory panel that includes Scott Rechler, the chairman of Reckson Associates, the real estate developer in Melville; Frank Castagna, who owns the Americana Shopping Plaza in Manhasset; and Jon Weinstein, the president of Apex Plastic Industries in Hauppauge.
The county might, for example, encourage employers by offering to defer sales tax for companies that agreed to hire workers from subsidized housing, he said.
More likely, the law of supply and demand will determine the outcome. By adding more housing, prices will drop, according to basic supply and demand theory. More workers will be able to live on Long Island, and that will make the region more appealing to employers.
Mr. Sylver challenged Dr. Koppelman's position that housing for the elderly failed to expand housing for the work force. "When retirees leave their houses, working families will be able to buy their homes and move in," Mr. Sylver said. "Plus retirees will be able to stay in the county and not leave."
Irwin Kellner, an economics professor at Hofstra University and the chairman of the county's Council of Economic Advisers, said that as far as he knew, the strategy of tying economic development to the construction of affordable housing was a Nassau County innovation.
"I have no idea if it's been done before, but it seems like a good idea," Dr. Kellner said. "Housing on Long Island in particular has jumped so rapidly in price that many people are priced out of the market. Yet the services those workers provide are needed by the economy."
Situating at least some of the new housing in commercial areas will also support downtown revitalization, one of six economic priorities set out last year by Mr. Suozzi. "For the most part, affordable housing will be in downtown areas, " Dr. Kellner said. "You fix up and build where the land is cheaper. You can't tell developers to put up housing at a loss. This being the case, they're going to put up affordable housing where values are lower. That tends to be in downtown areas."
Dr. Kellner said that this approach was economically justified and that it would also sidestep well-organized neighborhood opposition. "If you were to mandate putting up an apartment house in the middle of a two-acre suburban zone, you would get a negative reaction," he said. "But I don't sense you would if you put it in downtown or in an industrial area."
Neal Lewis, the executive director of the Long Island Neighborhood Network, a community advocacy organization, said he supported plans to tie downtown housing development with economic growth. But he added that in the past government-financed housing projects often failed because of poor planning.
"Years ago, there were so many housing mistakes," Mr. Lewis said. "You'd see a really big apartment house with hundreds of units. That's poor planning, and it overwhelms the community. You want to mix it up, 10 units here, 10 units there." 
Community opposition arises when subsidized housing in concentrated in low- and moderate-income areas, he said. Or as he put it: "I don't think we will see subsidized housing in Old Brookville." 
Dr. Koppelman, the regional planner, said that rental housing, rather than owner-occupied units, ought to be the priority, although he acknowledged that rentals represented a larger political risk. 
And the county's goals ought to be higher, he said, to make a measurable impact. "For the program to be meaningful, we need to be talking about several thousand housing units. And we're not talking about housing for people with entry-level, minimum-wage jobs. We're talking about affordable housing for engineers and scientists out of college."
"These are people earning $70,000 who can't afford to live and work in Nassau County," he said.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Raffaela Petrasek will head Nassau County's Economic Development Corporation, which will be instrumental in building or renovating affordable housing. (Phil Marino for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Aviation Museum Hopes to Strut Its Stuff 

BYLINE:  By VIVIAN S. TOY 

 BODY:
OFFICIALS at the Cradle of Aviation Museum love it when it rains.
Bad weather invariably means more tickets sold as families desperate to get out of the house find their way to the 130,000 square foot museum complex in East Garden City.
That's why Tom Gwynne, the museum's vice president of external affairs, recalls with mock bitterness that last summer, "it didn't rain a single day."
 But last year's drought was only one of the reasons why the museum has drawn just 240,000 visitors since it opened last May, an impressive number for a museum's first year, but well below the 400,000 visitors museum officials hoped to draw. 
 The museum was more than 30 years in the making, its opening continually delayed by fiscal crises and political infighting over the more than $40 million poured into it by Nassau County. So when the museum finally opened its doors last spring, it was still very much a work in progress -- an education director had not yet been hired and one of its key attractions, a simulated space voyage to Mars, had not yet been completed.
But perhaps more important, the $300,000 museum officials hoped to spend on advertising and marketing instead had to be largely diverted to help finish creating some of the museum's exhibits, which range from first attempts at flight in gliders and hot air balloons to World War II fighter planes and lunar test modules used in the United States' race to the moon. Promises from Albany to help market the museum along with other air and space attractions in the state also did not materialize until very recently.
The still-not-quite-finished museum chronicles Long Island's significant place in aviation history with interactive displays, model cockpits that allow children and adults to sit in a pilot's seat, and the island's only IMAX theater. 
Visitors of all ages seem impressed by the museum's scale and scope. "There's a lot more here than I thought there would be," said George Kinkel, a Levittown resident who visited the museum one rainy day last week with relatives from North Carolina and Vermont.
"I think we've basically accomplished most of our goals for the first year," said Edward J. Smits, the chief executive of Museums at Mitchel, the nonprofit agency created to run the museum.
Mr. Smits said the museum would come close to meeting a modified attendance goal of 300,000 for the fiscal year that began last July. He said museum officials had lowered their expectations to 300,000 because of "the events of 2002 and 2003," namely the recession, national security alerts and the war. He also argued that it made sense to not count the museum's first six weeks because "they were still very experimental days and not really typical for operation."
But Jane D'Amico, president of the Civil Service Employees Association, which unsuccessfully sued the county to try to stop the county's contract with Museums at Mitchel and instead have county employees run the museum, said the missed attendance goal is a sign that "sometimes giving something to a private concern is not the answer."
Given all the money the county invested in the museum, she said, operations should have stayed in county hands. "We should have kept it, used creative ways to make it shine and be a big attraction, instead of selling it off and wondering why it's not doing better," she said. "But unfortunately the county locked themselves into it and I think they'll be very sorry down the line." 
The operating agreement with Museums at Mitchel, which was formerly known as Nassau Heritage, originally had the county paying for the museum's utilities and receiving no revenues from admissions fees or concessions. But county legislators haggled with museum officials and finally settled on a 15-year lease where the county pays for some utilities and will receive a half percent of profits from 2005 to 2011, with the take rising to 3 percent after that.
The museum's $4 million budget for this last year is so far balanced, a feat that is "very positive for a museum open less than a year," said Mr. Gwynne, the museum vice president. "Long Islanders have been very receptive," he said, but the lack of any advertising budget has been a hindrance. "We need to get the message out and ideally we'd like to blanket the media and have newspaper and television ads to make sure Cradle of Aviation is a household word."
Next door, the Long Island Children's Museum finished its first year last February with attendance at 263,000. But the Children's Museum is a much smaller operation at only 40,000 square feet and began with a membership of 650 when it moved from an even smaller space in Garden City. The Children's Museum also had virtually no advertising budget, but managed to increase its membership nearly tenfold to more than 6,000. In many ways, though, a children's museum is a known and familiar entity, while an aviation museum is a new concept and a tougher sell.
The IMAX theater and the Mars space voyage are both big draws and as predicted, account for much of the museum's revenues. There are additional charges for the theater and the ride, and between 60 and 70 percent of the museum's visitors opt for at least one of them. 
The IMAX theater now runs two shows: The Magic of Flight and Space Station, but plans are under way for a summer film festival that will include films on thrill rides, extreme sports, helicopters, sharks and Michael Jordan.
Mr. Smits said a more extensive marketing plan also will be in place soon, including discount ticketing for certain times and arrangements with corporations like American Express to run advertisements in their promotional materials. Without an education director until late last year, the museum did not book any school groups for the fall, but expects to have a full reservation schedule with roughly 900 school children every day of the next school year. "
Richard Maloney, who brought his wife, daughter and grandchildren from Wantagh to the museum last week, said they had heard about the museum from a friend and were very impressed with what they saw. 
"I especially liked the people there because they answered all our questions and they were enthused," he said, referring to the volunteer docents, most of whom are retired from Long Island's aerospace industry.
Mr. Maloney's daughter, Tracy Masterson, said she didn't even realize that about half of the museum's galleries are unfinished, with planes essentially sitting in an open airplane hangar as opposed to carefully designed museum backdrops and panoramas.
"I didn't even notice that," she said. "And you know what, I wouldn't even change it."

HEADLINE: If You're Thinking of Living In/Millwood; 
Lush Landscape, and Chappaqua Schools

BYLINE:  By ELSA BRENNER 

 BODY:
AS young parents in 1972, Kay and Kenneth Schonberg were drawn to Millwood by the outstanding reputation of the local school system and the many recreational opportunities in the area for families. Active on the school board and in community organizations, they led a busy life as their son and daughter grew up. Now, while other empty nesters are moving away, the Schonbergs continue to live in the rustic hamlet, which is part of the town of New Castle in northern Westchester.
 "There's still plenty here for just the two of us," Mrs. Schonberg said recently. The couple -- she is a social worker and he is a pediatrician -- continue to enjoy walks along the tree-lined footpath that runs behind their four-bedroom colonial home. They grow vegetables in a garden in their backyard. And they still swim in the small lake at the end of their street, although these days they are teaching their grandchildren, not their children, to float. 
 By contrast, Rory and Melissa Lubin are typical of young couples now buying homes in Millwood. Lawyers in their early 30's, they moved last summer from a studio apartment on the Upper East Side of Manhattan to a three-bedroom house on a half-acre lot in the hamlet. They chose Millwood for many of the same reasons that Dr. and Mrs. Schonberg were drawn to it 31 years ago. 
Planning to start a family soon, the Lubins wanted to be part of the Chappaqua Central School District. Other reasons for moving to Millwood were the lushness of the local landscape and the area's broad offering of outdoor recreational activities.
"Compared to New York City, living up here is like being in another world," said Mr. Lubin, who works for Wilson, Elser, Moskowitz, Edelman & Dicker in White Plains. "When we look outside our windows now, we see woods instead of just more buildings. It's a sense of being removed from it all, even though we're really not that far away from work and cultural events."
Mr. Lubin commutes by car to his White Plains office, a trip that takes about 15 minutes; Mrs. Lubin's daily drive to the Bronx, where she is a Family Court lawyer, takes about 45 minutes.
Marion S. Sinek, the supervisor of New Castle, said that attracting a diversity of age groups to the town -- like those represented by the Schonbergs and the Lubins -- was a primary goal of her administration. 
In Millwood, 8 percent of the population is 5 or less; 20 percent is 6 to 17; 14 percent is 18 to 34; 54 percent is 35 toand 64; and 4 percent is 65 or older, according to the 1990 latest available United States census. Chappaqua's population follows a similar pattern. Mrs. Sinek wants to change those skewed distributions reflecting the fact that people tend to move elsewhere after 65. 
"One way to get a better balance," Mrs. Sinek said, "is to keep taxes down so that not just young families, but older couples as well, can live here." Toward that end, New Castle held its tax increase this year to less than 5 percent, which she characterized as good during tough economic times. (Residents pay separate town and school district taxes.)
New Castle governs residents living in the hamlets of Millwood and most of Chappaqua, as well as some with mailing addresses in Armonk, Briarcliff, Briarcliff Manor, Mount Kisco, Ossining and Yorktown, according to the town clerk, Linda Peterson, who also collects the taxes.
Millwood, although in some ways quite similar to Chappaqua, tends to have smaller houses on smaller lots and less name recognition than the much larger Chappaqua. Few houses in Millwood come on the market each year. For example, only 21 homes there were sold last year. "No one ever comes into our office asking specifically for Millwood," said David Firnhaber, the manager of Houlihan/Lawrence's office in Chappaqua. "Like the Lubins, what they want is a house in the Chappaqua Central School District."
CHILDREN living in Millwood -- which makes up ZIP code 10546 -- attend Westorchard Elementary School, which serves 573 students in kindergarten through fifth grade.
In 2002, 97 percent of fourth graders at Westorchard scored at competency level or above on the state English language arts exams, with 64 percent of those reaching Level 4, the highest level. On the fourth-grade mathematics test, 96 percent scored at competency level or above last year, with 63 percent at the highest level, said Westorchard's principal, William Huppuch.
All Millwood students also attend Chappaqua's Horace Greeley High School, where last year, 18 students were National Merit Scholar finalists. Of the 260 seniors who graduated in the spring of 2002, 95 percent now attend four-year colleges and 2 percent attend two-year institutions. Average scores for students from Horace Greeley taking SAT exams last year were 604 on the verbal section and 638 on the math section, said Dennis Cuddy, chairman of the high school's counseling department, far above the statewide average scores of 494 on the verbal test and 506 in math.
Per-pupil spending for the district, which now has three elementary schools, one middle school and the high school, is $16,289, said Henne Starace, the district's business administrator. The ratio of teachers to students varies throughout the district, she said, with kindergarten classes never having more than 20 students.
The district's school population has been growing steadily in the last decade, with enrollment currently at 4,055. The number was 2,866 in 1990 and is expected to peak at 4,298 in 2007, said the superintendent of schools, James F. Donovan. Growing school enrollments prompted the construction of a second middle school, the $32.5 million, 160,000-square-foot Seven Bridges Middle School, which will open next fall for 650 students in the fifth through eighth grades.
Dr. Donovan said he was grateful for "the legacy of support for education" in the school district, with residents "highly supportive of school spending." Last year voters approved a $66.05 million school budget; this year's proposed budget is $74.6 million.
The Chappaqua Central School District Public Library has not been so fortunate with its plans to expand. Three years ago, voters rejected a proposed $8.5 million renovation package that would have increased space in the 25,000-square-foot library by 75 percent. "Instead, we added shelving everywhere we could, except between the front door and the circulation desk," said Mark Hasskarl, the library director.
In the past, houses in Millwood were not as expensive as those in Chappaqua, especially since it was the less-developed of the two hamlets, with fewer homes and shops. It is also four miles from the Chappaqua stop on Metro-North's Harlem Line. Today, however, prices are about the same in both hamlets, said Elvira Bruno, who owns the real estate firm of Howe & Sibley in downtown Chappaqua.
In many ways, Millwood has always been the slower sister of Chappaqua. As far back at the 1800's, Millwood took longer to develop as a residential area, according to Al Hutin, president of the New Castle historical society. For years, it was little more than a stagecoach stop between the Bowery in Lower Manhattan and Somers.
When the New York Central Railroad brought its Putnam Division to Millwood, the hamlet finally blossomed as an agricultural market. Before the 1880's, farmers had to take their produce to Ossining or Chappaqua, the latter having rail service on New York Central's Harlem Division since the 1840's, the Historical Society's records show.
Chappaqua became a commuter town about 1900, with Millwood following a decade later, Mr. Hutin said.
In recent years, the disparity between the two hamlets has begun to lessen. Especially in the last 15 to 20 years, residential development in both hamlets has been intense, with all parts of the Chappaqua Central School District attracting well-heeled families and New Castle becoming known in real estate circles as "Scarsdale North," Mrs. Bruno at Howe & Sibley said.
ABOUT one-fourth of Millwood's land belongs to public utilities like Con Ed, the state Department of Transportation and New York City for reservoirs, said Frank Annunziata, a town engineer. "Because of that," Mr. Hutin, the historian, said, "Millwood has more undeveloped land and doesn't appear to be as crowded as some neighboring areas like Mount Kisco, Chappaqua, Pleasantville, Briarcliff or Ossining."
And while Millwood residents treasure those undeveloped open spaces, many in recent years have sought at least a modicum of commercial development, said Sarah Brewster, a past chairwoman of the Millwood Task Force, an appointed advisory body formed by the town in 1983. "There were no sidewalks, just a limited number of stores, and nothing cohesive for shopping," said Mrs. Brewster, who was also chairman of New Castle's planning board. "So we pushed for more development."
Today, the immediate shopping area on Route 100 near Route 133 that once contained just a few stores near an A.&P. has been expanded, with two more small shopping centers added nearby, along with sidewalks and streetlamps.
One of the longtime favorite restaurants in the hamlet is Rocky's Millwood Delicatessen. Owned by Greg Santone for 23 years, the deli is a 24/7 operation serving Rocky's sandwiches and its trademark burgers to customers, many of whom are scientists and mathematicians seeking a break during long nights working at I.B.M. in nearby Yorktown.
Having achieved the level of commercial development recommended by the task force, most residents now focus on preserving the small-town qualities that remain. "What we want is to maintain the feeling of community that was here when people first started moving to the area," Mrs. Sinek said.
"What we don't like is when new people come in and try to change things too much. That doesn't happen too often, but unfortunately there are always those few who move here because they love the way the area looks, and then, for example, they want to cut down trees so they can have a bigger lawn. That's not in the best interests of either of our hamlets."

CORRECTION-DATE: May 18, 2003

 CORRECTION:
An article on April 27 about living in Millwood, N.Y., misstated the year in which a local advisory board, the Millwood Task Force, was formed. The board, whose former chairwoman was quoted as saying it had advocated some increased commercial development, dates from 1974, not 1983.

Sinek, Democrat), elected to two-year term; four Town Council members elected to staggered two-year terms.THE GRANITE HOUSE: In the early 1800's, the Croton turnpike ran southeast through Somers and what is now Millwood to Sing Sing, with farmers stopping at an inn known as the Granite House. The Greek Revival building was built on the site by David and Rachael Crasto between 1814 and 1817, records show, with 18-inch-thick granite blocks quarried a nearby rock ledge, now under power lines near the railroad track. In the early-to-mid 1800's land auctions were held at the Granite House, and it also served as a polling place for New Castle. The building and 35 acres were sold in 1817 for $3,200 to William Sarles, deeds show, and the area now known as Millwood was called Sarles' Corners. During the 1850's it was the first stagecoach stop between White Plains and Somers and was also on runs from the Bowery to Somers. The Sarles family sold the property to the Merritt family, which also ran it as an inn until the early 1900's, and the name of the area changed to Merritt's Corners. It changed hands and in the 1920's became a speakeasy. It was demolished in 1992. Map of New York highlighting Millwood.

HEADLINE: Big-City Buildings Seek A Small-Town Feeling 

BYLINE:  By NADINE BROZAN 

 BODY:
THOUGH New Yorkers have long had the reputation -- deserved or not -- of savoring their anonymity and protecting their privacy, many of them are seeking community within the four walls of their own buildings. From the transformation of a barren lot into a trellised gathering spot behind a six-story co-op to a 13,000-square-foot fitness and social center in a 53-story rental, from evening concerts to bedtime stories for groups of children, examples of facilities and programs meant to foster neighborly connections in urban settings abound. 
 Take, for example, the St. Tropez at 340 East 64th Street, the first high rise in the state to be built as a condominium. There are more than half a dozen movie theaters within walking distance, to say nothing of VCR's, DVD's and satellite television, but the board recently bought equipment to show movies in the party room. And when Charlie Chaplin's "Gold Rush" was shown, a pianist was brought in to play accompanying ragtime. 
 "We have 300 apartments in this building and want to keep a sense of cohesiveness," said Robert Metz, who is in charge of movies and edits the house's newsletter. 
The St. Tropez, built in 1965, also gets people together through body sculpting classes (five times a week), yoga, brunches and its rooftop swimming pool, which is ringed by lounge chairs. "When you know your neighbor, you're likely to be more respectful of them," said Suzie Schuyler, a board member who oversees both the fitness and welcoming committees.
Indeed, there is growing acknowledgment on the part of shareholders, owners, tenants, boards and developers that city dwellers increasingly want at least part of their social and athletic needs filled within the confines of their own buildings. To one degree or another, many nests are becoming villages. 
"Our attitude is that we are a family unit rather than isolated units," said Guy I. Smiley, a lawyer and president of the board of the Sovereign, a 352-apartment co-op at 425 East 58th Street. "We use the word 'co-op' as a signature of our philosophy. After 9/11, I think everyone was looking for a connection and home, and where you live is a great place to have that."
His definition of family is inclusive. "We have a lot of dogs in the building, and animals are members of our family, too," he said. 
According to Costas Kondylis, who has designed more than 45 residential buildings in Manhattan since 1958, "People like to feel that when they come home they are part of a club. This gives you an identity, a meeting place, a sense of community. For the rent you are paying these days, you expect more than the four walls of the apartment."
Setha Low, a professor of environmental psychology and anthropology at the City University of New York Graduate Center and the author of a new book on gated communities, read a darker reason into the trend. "Being worried about security is changing the nature of urban life," she said. "We are fearful, anxious, insecure and nostalgic for the past, and so we are becoming more insular." 
WHATEVER the underlying motives, allotting generous space for health clubs and leisure-use rooms is now considered mandatory for residential design. 
"These amenities are essential for any major building," said James Davidson, a partner at Schuman Lichtenstein Claman Efron Architects. "At a minimum, you must have a party room and media center where residents and friends can get together for, say, the Super Bowl, and a health center or workout room." Beyond that, his firm is designing basketball courts, putting greens and children's playrooms for inclusion in buildings. 
But offering up-to-the-minute athletic equipment and a busy schedule of social and cultural events represents not only concern for the residents' well-being; it is also smart marketing.
"It's nice to walk into a lobby and be thanked for what you do rather than be accosted for what you don't," said David Pickett, president of the Gotham Organization. His firm has developed four large rental buildings: the New Gotham at 43rd Street between 10th and 11th Avenues, the Key West at 96th Street and Columbus Avenue, the Foundry at 55th Street and 10th Avenue and the Atlas, which opened at Sixth Avenue and 38th Street in July.
"This helps us rent and retain tenants," he said. "If on every apartment I can get an extra $50 or $100, I can engender good will and long-term patronage. And when leases come up and you're negotiating with tenants, the hope is that they like living there and there is something other than economic factors tugging at them."
The Jack Parker Corporation, developer and manager of the Biltmore, a new 53-story rental at 271 West 47th Street, has devoted the entire fifth floor, more than 13,000 square feet, to what Kimberly Cafaro, a vice president and general manager, calls "the fitness element and the social element." 
"We felt we had to go above and beyond what other owners are doing to draw people to the building," Ms. Cafaro said. In addition to the standard assortment of exercise machines, it also has billiard tables, dartboards, a business center, a lounge with a fireplace, a screening room that converts to an aerobics and yoga center and a kitchen that will provide "breakfast on the go" of rolls, muffins or pastry to be handed to health club members (the health club is $400 a year).
In older buildings, whose original blueprints did not take the demand for communal facilities into account, existing facilities have been upgraded or altered. 
A gym was opened in December at the Riviera Towers, a 34-story co-op in West New York, N.J., that overlooks Manhattan 
"It was an offshoot of a lot of requests from residents," said Anthony Errante, secretary of the co-op's board. "We have an outdoor pool where people meet in the summer, but there is not a lot in the winter. 
"We also felt strongly about creating a larger space where we could hold events. We have a small community room where we have open board meetings, but it is not large enough for events." 
When the building was put up in the 1960's, space was set aside below grade for retail stores, but none were built. "So we had a lot of underutilized space," Mr. Errante said. "The decision was made to turn part of that into a community level and now there is a activities committee to organize events like concerts there. We also had a flea market when it opened."
In some places, the laundry room has acquired a new identity.
"We decided to make it a comfortable place to relax," A. J. Mediratta, president of the board at the 100-unit co-op at 35 West 90th Street, said of the room there. "We put patio furniture in it and a number of bookshelves so people can drop off and borrow books. Last year someone put in tables and earlier this year hung pictures.
"We tend to spend so much on things that people never see, like rebuilding parapets and lintels, that it is nice to spend a small amount on things that are visible. I think these little things foster a better atmosphere. You get more out of living here if you have friends, if you have someone to keep an eye on the goldfish when you go traveling." 
Similarly, in 1999 an exercise room was carved out of bicycle and laundry rooms at the two connected buildings that constitute the co-op at 110 and 118 Riverside Drive. 
"We did the whole thing over very simply," said Laurie Winfrey Shnayerson, treasurer of the board, who has a picture editing and research company. "We kept the existing columns, put up mirrors and new lighting and put in a floor of reprocessed tires." 
There was a one-time fee of $3,000 to join with a charge of $25 per month, and the loan to alter the space was repaid in three years. 
"People talk to each other when they're sweating on the treadmills and watching CNN," Ms. Shnayerson said. "You see people there you would never know otherwise and there is the recognition factor when you see someone dressed on the street."
Nearby at 316 West 84th Street, a 141-unit, six-story co-op, an expansion into an unused rear lot has fostered a friendlier ambience among the shareholders. 
"When we co-oped, probably 20 years ago, we had a backyard that was all cracked and no one ever used," said Mort Berkowitz, a board member whose company, Mort & Ray Productions, runs street fairs. "About eight years ago, we repaved and now have a garden, trellises, vines, lounge chairs and tables. There is nothing nicer in the summer than sitting out there with coffee or wine and cheese."
More than a dozen events have been held in the 20-by-100-foot garden in the last year, ranging from bar mitzvah receptions to anniversary celebrations. "It makes everything more civil," Mr. Berkowitz said. "In the past, the only people you knew were the ones on your floor. Now there are communities of interest, and lots of people have become friendly across floors."
A full menu of activities has long been an integral part of life at Stuyvesant Town and Peter Cooper Village, the 110-building community that runs from East 14th to East 23rd Street, "but we are continuing to enhance our programs," said Steve Stadmeyer, the managing director of the complex. 
With a full-time recreation staff of 15, he said, "we are adding four boccie courts; adult volleyball; more computers in the senior lounge; two new playgrounds, one for very small children and one for peewee soccer; and a Starbuck's to provide free coffee on Saturday morning. Two years ago we began having summer concerts on Tuesday evening specifically to bring people together." 
At North Shore Towers and Country Club, a three-building, 1,800-unit co-op on the Queens-Nassau County border in Floral Park, "what you get is a lifestyle, not just an apartment," said Linda Rappaport, the on-site sales specialist for the Charles H. Greenthal brokerage firm. "It has a 460-seat movie theater, a restaurant, catering hall, outdoor hot-dog stand, and clubhouse where breakfast and lunch are available."
Sitting on a 110-acre site and bordered by an 18-hole golf course, North Shore Towers has extensive athletic offerings, some of them commanding separate fees that go from $800 for use of the swimming pools and health club to $3,200 for all activities, including golf. Social events on the calendar last month included bingo, ballroom dancing and lectures on subjects as diverse as Picasso and "Dealing with Aches and Pains." 
More than 300 residents and their relatives attended seders in the restaurant there on the first two nights of Passover earlier this month.
"In most cases, the residents are the ones who develop and implement all these things," said Phyllis Goldstein, a board member who is in charge of the enhancement committee. "That means I do pretty things. 
"This is small-town living," she added, "and when you interact in the club, the movie theater, the coffee shop, over the years, you get to know everybody." 
By contrast, the stately co-op buildings on Park and Fifth Avenues tend to have a bicycle room or perhaps an exercise room but not much more to promote camaraderie between strangers. 
"They wouldn't get into the building if they didn't already know their neighbors or were at least friends of friends," said Kirk Henckels, the director of Stribling Private Brokerage. "These people want service, room service." (The buildings at 960 Fifth Avenue and 1 East 66th Street have chefs on the premises to meet that need.) 
The co-op at 825 Fifth Avenue does have a dining room that is used by many tenants, but Caroline Guthrie, executive vice president of Edward Lee Cave, a high-end Manhattan brokerage, explained: "Generally the apartments are pieds-a-terre, so most of the kitchens are kitchenettes. The dining room is only open Monday through Friday because people are at their country homes on weekends." 
Hollis Court, a complex of 18 two-family garden co-ops in Bayside, Queens, was once a bastion of group activity, but according to Patricia Brown, a retired registered nurse who recently stepped down as president, interest has dropped off dramatically. 
"We had a bowling night, but it wasn't successful," she said. "We used to have a gardening committee, a social room for cards and mah-jongg, a lending library, an annual barbecue. This year people don't seem to be interested in barbecues and they want their gardening done." 
An owner of 19 years, Ms. Brown attributed the change to the fact that "people who lived here for many years made a killing in the real estate market and moved out and the younger people who are moving in are professionals and singles who are totally caught up in their careers; it's hard to get people together because they lead such busy lives." 
The Gotham Organization has tried to address that issue with some of the programs that it gears to the young singles who predominate in several of its buildings.
BASICALLY each of our buildings has its own personality, but several of them tend to have young professional people who go to hip restaurants and frequent nightclubs," said Lindsay Levine, social director for the organization. "So some of these events are great things do do from say 8:30 to 10:30 before they go out.
"People really do connect, though not necessarily romantically. They tend to talk about the building, and one of our worries was that it would become a forum to voice complaints, but that hasn't happened." 
Although Gotham's events are geared to the building in which they are held, they are open to the residents of all 1,300 apartments in the four buildings and range from tapas parties with mariachi bands to sushi tastings, concerts, art exhibits and bedtime stories for young children. 
At the Key West on 96th Street and Columbus Avenue, "we gear a lot of events to kids," Ms. Levine said. "I met a graduate of Juilliard who specializes in children's entertainment and comes for what we call 'bedtime stories.' " On her occasional visits, "She brings bags of tricks, balloons, puppets and storybooks and we put out milk and cookies at bedtime."
A growing number of buildings are keeping their residents informed and connected through regular newsletters.
"Manhattan can be a cold place, and the idea is to build esprit de corps," said Bernard Bushell, an investment banker who is the editor of the newsletter that was introduced in January at the Sovereign, the co-op at 425 East 58th Street. 
The Sovereign Sentinel, which will come out quarterly, is a blend of real estate savvy -- a chart in the first issue tracked the history of sales since 1998 -- and small-town neighborliness like the "Share a Recipe" column. 
The first two issues also had profiles of the doormen and the garage manager, a review of a neighborhood restaurant, and a guide to eating fine chocolate from a resident in the business -- "Taste on an empty stomach. Clear palate with filtered water ... chew seven to 10 times ... allowing secondary flavors such as herbal, fruity or spicy to emerge."
Requests in print included ones for volunteers for a caregivers' club to help the elderly in the building and a children's club, along with referrals for personal trainers and computer consultants, and the name of a social secretary who will introduce new residents around. 
One of the folksier inclusions is news and photographs of pets in the building. (Two black lab puppies have taken up residence in apartment 47B, the spring issue announced.) 
But, as always, there is a distinctly Manhattan twist. "One resident had a public relations consultant call me to get their dog in the paper," Mr. Bushell said.

HEADLINE: Harmony in Hartford: Play On 

BYLINE:  By BRIAN WISE 

 BODY:
LIKE many American orchestras, the Hartford Symphony Orchestra is expected to post a deficit at the end of this season, because this year its endowment has lost much of its value and financing from state arts organizations has dropped off.
 Still, orchestra officials say the overall health of the ensemble is good, with strong ticket sales for core classical and pops concerts; a cordial relationship between management and musicians; and a promising series of programming changes under a new music director, Edward Cumming. 
 "I often say it's both the best and the worst of times," said Charles Owens, the group's executive director. "We're excited by the start of a new era with the arrival of Edward Cumming, but at the same time, we're facing some real hurdles with the drop in our endowment." 
The financial books on the orchestra's 59th season will not be officially closed until late spring. But Mr. Owens said the weakened economy and plummeting portfolios had eroded the endowment to $7.7 million from a high of $10 million in 2000.
Additionally, financing from the Greater Hartford Arts Council and the Connecticut Commission on the Arts has declined slightly. Both of those sources of income are used to supplement ticket sales andcontributions.
The cash crunch forced the orchestra to make several midseason adjustments to bring its expenses under control. The pops concerts, which ran on Friday and Saturday nights, were consolidated into a single Saturday night series.
The orchestra also delayed the hiring for several staff positions, and moved its eight-part education series from the high-rent Bushnell Center for the Performing Arts in Hartford, the orchestra's main home, to more modest surroundings at Central Connecticut State University in New Britain.
The orchestra has also continued to take on some crossover projects that have met with criticism from critics and classical music purists. In 2001, the group accompanied Andrea Bocelli, the blind tenor and pop phenomenon, on a national tour that included stops in arenas and the Bellagio hotel in Las Vegas.
Last February, the orchestra presented the First Symphony of Albert Hurwit, a retired Hartford radiologist without any formal musical training. The work was lambasted in the local news media as amateurish and cliched. 
Mr. Owens said Mr. Hurwit's symphony was chosen, in part, because it made for a likable human-interest story because he composed with the assistance of an elaborate keyboard and computer system.
"Mr. Hurwit is a respected member of the Hartford community," Mr. Owens said. Mr. Hurwit and his wife, Joan, are prominent arts patrons in Hartford.
No programming changes for next year have been announced thus far, and unlike many orchestras suffering from the weak economy, the symphony has not asked its musicians to take a pay cut. Their contract was renewed for three years last July. The players' salaries are 36 percent of the symphony's $5.1million budget. 
Budget figures suggest that the public has responded enthusiastically to the arrival of Mr. Cumming, 44, who was selected in 2001 from more than 280 applicants. He previously held posts at the Pittsburgh Symphony and Florida Orchestra, and studied at Yale University. 
Indeed, the number of subscribers to the symphony has increased 16 percent from last season, and subscription revenue is up by 12 percent. There has been a slight increase in single-ticket sales, and an average concert in the Masterworks series, the group's core classical program, can claim a 65 percent capacity, no small feat at the 2,800-seat Mortensen Hall at the Bushnell.
Connecticut arts administrators say the orchestra's financial problems appear to be largely manageable. Ken Kahn, executive director of the arts council, said the orchestra was reaching out to previously untapped segments of the city with a more diverse programming mix.
"The symphony is taking great pains to create formats that are probably not unique in the world, but whether it's an early evening concert or concerts in parks, they build the presence of the symphony itself throughout the community," he said. 
Specifically, Mr. Kahn pointed to the newly created Rush Hour Classics series in the Belding Theater, which has been playing to more than 90 percent capacity. With its 6:30 p.m. start times, cocktail receptions, and 75-minute, conversation-and-performance format led by Mr. Cumming, the concerts target a young professional demographic. 
Mr. Kahn described the orchestra's Latino Music Festival last October, and the annual summer concert series at Talcott Mountain Music Festival in Simsbury as "wildly successful," adding that "they play to all spectrums of society here which is very good." 
But series like these attract more casual concertgoers than core classical subscribers, and that kind ofaudience is more susceptible to the vagaries of the economy. To remain fiscally strong, the orchestra must lure more people to its main programs in Hartford.
Mr. Owens said the two top-selling concerts this season were performances of Beethoven's seldom-performed Mass in C Major and the North American premiere of Stephen Montague's Piano Concerto, an extravagant work that calls for the pianist to use his fist, arm and elbow on the keyboard. 
For its final subscription concerts of the season, May6 and 7 at the Bushnell, Mr. Cumming will conduct Mahler's Symphony No.5 along with works by Anton Webern and Aaron Jay Kernis.
"It would be very easy to program a Mozart piano concerto in the first half of a program containing Mahler's Fifth," Mr. Cumming said. "But you can do more with that space on the program. 
Although the orchestra plainly does not shy away from adventuresome programs, crowd-pleasing fare is a staple; its second-largest annual series is the pops concerts at the Bushnell.
Mr. Owens acknowledged that the orchestra's main focus is the standard repertory.
"We don't expect to make any drastic changes in our programming next season," he said.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Budget woes aside, the Hartford Symphony, under the direction of Edward Cumming, is hopeful. (Thomas McDonald for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: To Get a Job, Become a Boss: The Lure of Owning a Franchise 

BYLINE:  By SHARON McDONNELL 

 BODY:
Despite 30 years in the retail clothing industry in Manhattan, including 13 at Bloomingdale's, where he rose to vice president for merchandising in the men's department, and 7 at the Custom Shop, Tony Cocheo found his career unraveling.
 In April 2001, his job as sales director at Tradeout.com, an online merchandiser, ended when the company went out of business. He then had the misfortune to be hired shortly before Sept. 11, 2001, as an assistant manager at the Bay Ridge, Brooklyn, store of the Century 21 department store chain. The terrorist attacks shut Century 21's flagship store across the street from the World Trade Center, and Mr. Cocheo was laid off a few weeks later. 
 So Mr. Cocheo, 58, took a different tack: he bought himself a job. In November 2001, he used "several hundred thousand dollars" in savings to become owner of a 7-Eleven convenience-store franchise in Southold on Long Island. "The world has changed; it was time for me to make a change," he said.
Mr. Cocheo is an example of what could be called the accidental entrepreneurs, people who buy a small business or a franchise rather than put themselves at the mercy of an unforgiving job market. The market is especially unforgiving in New York City, which has lost about 223,000 jobs in the last two years and had an unemployment rate of 8.8 percent in February, compared with 4.3 percent in the rest of the state and 5.8 percent nationally.
While born entrepreneurs relish risk, these more-reluctant entrepreneurs choose business ownership because it often seems less daunting than working for somebody else. Many turn to franchises, which can cushion the risk-taking by offering a brand name, established business methods, training, manuals, advertising help, a support network, financial help and bookkeeping.
The International Franchise Association, a Washington-based trade group, estimates that there are 1,500 franchisers in the country with a total of about 320,000 outlets. They range from hotel chains, which require multimillion-dollar investments, and giant fast-food chains like McDonald's and Krispy Kreme, which can cost $500,000 and up, to providers of drug-sniffing dogs, bail bonds, dating services and check cashing. While about 80 percent of franchises require an initial total investment below $250,000, nearly half cost less than $100,000, and some much less.
Experts caution that buying and running a franchise can be tricky. Investors should be aware of the many expenses involved, which usually include a flat franchise fee, payment to the franchiser of a percentage of revenue, as well as costs related to real estate, construction, inventory and advertising. All of which is compounded by the fact that many people opening new franchise outlets are also new to owning a business. One study even suggested the failure rate for franchise outlets is higher than for independent businesses in the same industry.
Susan P. Kezios, president of the American Franchisee Association, which has 7,000 members who own over 30,000 franchise outlets, has few kind words for franchisers, saying many prey on people who have lost their jobs in hard times and often concoct agreements that are loaded against unsuspecting franchisees.
For example, she says, some provisions allow the franchise company to add competing outlets nearby at will, while also including noncompete agreements limiting the franchisee's right to engage in a similar business after the contract ends. Typically, the franchisee must agree to use preferred suppliers and accept conditions for contract renewals that differ radically in terms of money and operations from the original and require arbitration in disputes in the franchise company's home state.
Ms. Kezios scoffs at the notion that buying a franchise is like buying a job. "It's more like renting an apartment, because it's a limited period of time and after the 10-, 15- or 20-year contract ends, the franchise corporation will rewrite it in their favor," she said.
The I.F.A., which represents both franchisers and franchisees, says those accusations are overblown. "Human nature is entitled to make mistakes -- sometimes bad decisions are made by the franchiser or franchisee," said Don DeBolt, president of the I.F.A., which has an ombudsman to resolve disputes.
The organization advises those considering opening a franchise outlet to evaluate the marketplace for high-growth areas, compare several franchise systems in the same industry, read federally mandated disclosure documents carefully and have both a lawyer and accountant trained in franchising review the contract and company. It also recommends contacting former and current franchisees. "If you hear tales of woe, I'd look elsewhere," he said.
Whatever the risks, a lot of job seekers seem to find them worth taking. Net growth in franchising this year is projected to be 4 percent to 5 percent, strong in this economy, but down from about 12 percent in 2000, according to Franchise Recruiters, an executive search firm in Chicago.
Subway International, which has franchised 15,000 fast-food shops in the United States, more than any other company, says it sold 4,300 last year alone, up from 2,500 in 2001. One-third of its 159 shops in New York City opened in the last two years, the company says.
One of Subway's recruits is John Biscaino, who served for 10 years in the Navy and then took a part-time job as a test pilot for the Sikorsky Aircraft Corporation in Stratford, Conn. He had planned to become an airline pilot, but put that ambition aside because of the industry's slump. Last year, he bought a Subway franchise in Park Slope, Brooklyn.
"This was Plan B, which became Plan A," said Mr. Biscaino, 33, who owns the franchise with his half brother, Robert Vidal, 24, a salesman on leave from I.B.M.
The two men put in $35,000 each and obtained a bank loan for the rest of the $150,000 investment. They say business is booming.
Despite the positive reaction from Mr. Biscaino and Mr. Vidal, Subway has fought a relatively large number of legal battles over the years with franchisees and landlords.
Refugees from another troubled industry, information technology, also have been gravitating toward franchising. Susana Ramos, 56, a computer services engineer in New York for the last 18 years, took a buyout from the Hewlett-Packard Company in June and bought a Computer Troubleshooters USA franchise in October for $10,400. She operates the business, which provides technical service to small and home-based businesses in a section of the Upper East Side, from her Queens apartment.
"Since my company was downsizing 15,000 people, I decided, 'Why not?' " Ms. Ramos said.
Apparently, in this economy, a lot of unemployed computer whizzes are in the same boat she was. James Greenfield, regional director for the New York metropolitan area for Computer Troubleshooters, which is based in Decatur, Ga., said his company's franchises in New York City and New Jersey had increased to 15 from 2 in the last two years.
"Almost all our franchisees are laid off from I.T., or anticipating it," Mr. Greenfield said. "They all see the handwriting on the wall."

GRAPHIC: Photo: John Biscaino said buying a Subway franchise in Park Slope, Brooklyn, was a "Plan B, which became Plan A." (Chester Higgins Jr./The New York Times)

HEADLINE: ROAD AND RAIL; 
A Train to the Games

BYLINE:  By GEORGE JAMES 

DATELINE: HACKENSACK 

 BODY:
MAYBE someday travelers will take a train to the Meadowlands complex instead of having to crawl along Route 3. But don't get in line to buy a ticket just yet, because there is a problem: money is scarcer than profits at the Continental Airlines Arena. 
 One estimate puts the cost of a rail link at nearly $200 million, at a time when the state is struggling with a $5 billion budget shortfall, New Jersey Transit is using capital funds for operating expenses and the state transportation trust fund is running low. 
 In addition, the state has had to assume the full cost of another once-promising transportation project: the $1 billion light-rail line from Trenton to Camden -- begun in the Whitman administration -- which has come in $400 million over budget and has been roundly criticized as a boondoggle. 
Nonetheless, officials from government, business, the transit agency and the New Jersey Sports and Exposition Authority insist that after decades of talk about a rail link to the Meadowlands in East Rutherford, momentum appears to be building for construction of one -- despite the state's desperate financial condition.
Two major projects are seen as giving impetus: Xanadu, the $1.3 billion family entertainment center at the Continental Arena site that is expected to begin construction in the summer, and the phased-in fall opening of the $450 million Secaucus transfer station, about five miles away, that will link 11 of the state's 12 rail lines and provide quick access to Manhattan for riders from northern and southern New Jersey.
"I'm encouraged by the dialogue taking place among all the politicians and business leaders, and I think there is consensus building in terms of what to look at in terms of a plan," said George Zoffinger, president of the sports authority. 
George D. Warrington, executive director of New Jersy Transit, told state legislators and business leaders at an Assembly committee hearing two weeks ago that his agency was looking into building a railroad spur, two to three miles long, that would link the Pascack Valley and Bergen County commuter lines to the sports complex. 
He also said the new link could be extended to serve a separate project consisting of four golf courses, hotels, office buildings and housing units being built on landfill farther south of the sports complex. He expects to have a detailed plan within six months.
Asked about the reason for his optimism given the state's dire economic outlook, he cited several factors.
"The Secaucus transfer hub is a reality, a natural anchor in the Meadowlands which collects in one point in very close proximity to the sports complex markets from all over New Jersey as well as Manhattan," Mr. Warrington said. "No.2, you have a development project that is much more extensive and attractive in terms of demand than just a sporting facility."
The details of how a rail passenger would get to the sports complex have to be worked out, said Jack Kanarek, senior director of project development at New Jersey Transit, but he said those traveling from Hoboken or Secaucus would probably be able to take the Bergen County line to the sports complex without having to transfer. Those coming from the north, he said, might also be able to ride directly to the site, or transfer for the ride on the spur, or ride several miles south to the Secaucus transfer and then take the Bergen County line north to the complex.
"Those are all the options on the table," Mr. Kanarek said. "It's too early to say what will work and what won't." 
Extending the light-rail system being built in Hudson and Bergen Counties into the area -- once considered a possibility -- now seems less practical. One estimate put the cost at $500 million. 
Some critics contend that a mass transit plan should have been a condition all along, most notably during negotiations with prospective developers of the Continental Arena site. The winning team of the Mills Corporation of Arlington, Va., and the Mack-Cali Realty Corporation of Cranford was chosen in February. 
Before being selected as the developers, they offered $65 million for infrastructure improvements, though most of that is marked for road projects around the complex, including improvements to Route 120. 
Assemblywoman Rose Marie Heck, a Republican from Hasbrouck Heights, complained recently: "The mass transportation piece should have been done prior to anyone being chosen as a developer. And no development should begin unless or until we have the mass transportation piece beginning simultaneously." 
Despite Ms. Heck's qualms, Mr. Zoffinger said construction on Xanadu -- which is expected to open in three years and be completed by 2009 -- will not be delayed by a lack of funds for mass transit. Mills officials did not respond to questions.
The way Ms. Heck and many others see it, Mills-Mack-Cali -- who will reap considerable financial rewards from Xanadu -- should pay for the rail link, but the team has already promised an upfront payment of $160 million that would eliminate the arena's longterm debt and is not seen as likely to give more. 
Martin Robins, director of the Voorhees Transportation Policy Institute of the Edward J. Bloustein School of Planning and Public Policy at Rutgers, said that although the plan for the rail spur seemed like a good one, the financial picture was "very cloudy." 
But Representative Steve Rothman, a Hackensack Democrat who is a member of the House Appropriations Committee and a new member of its transportation subcommittee, said the rail link would qualify for federal funds, which would in turn encourage financing from other sources.
"This is an extraordinary piece of property," he said. "In order to maximize the value and potential benefits of this redeveloped Meadowlands property, rail is going to have to be incorporated into the project."
Earlier this year, Governor McGreevey said the state was $5 billion in the hole for its transportation improvement projects. He created a panel headed by Transportation Commissioner Jack Lettiere to set priorities and find sources of funds.
"What has to come out of this process is something of a rationale for how these projects are going to be put in rank and a financing scheme to accomplish them," Mr. Robins said. "And it may be we can't do all of the things we want to do."
Nonetheless, Dennis F. Marco, chairman of the Meadowlands Regional Chamber of Commerce and a member of the advisory committee that studied redevelopment plans for the arena site, said he was confident that a united front of government officials and business leaders would get the rail link built.
"You know how the planets line up every 700 years?" he said. "Well, this is our 700-year shot."

HEADLINE: FIVE QUESTIONS for ROBERT A. KAPP; 
Tracking the Impact of SARS on Business With China

BYLINE:  By RICK GLADSTONE 

 BODY:
 THOUSANDS of American business people have canceled travel to Asia, particularly to China, because of the spread of severe acute respiratory syndrome, or SARS. But broader economic shock waves from the disease have barely begun to resonate in the United States, which is China's second-largest trading partner, behind Japan, and one of China's biggest sources of foreign investment. 
 Robert A. Kapp, president of the U.S.-China Business Council in Washington, the principal organization of American corporations that do business in China, spoke on Wednesday about short-term and potential long-term effects of SARS.
 RICK GLADSTONE

Q. Have any large business deals between American companies and China been delayed or derailed because of SARS?

A. We are not aware of any large transactions which have fallen through over this. But the fact that nobody's traveling, sooner or later, has got to have an impact. The longer this goes on, the more likely the final consummations are going to be delayed. We don't expect a flood of deals while this situation remains.

Q. Which industries that do business in China have been hurt most? Which have been relatively insulated?

A. Obviously, airlines are at the top of the list. We're not seeing the autos, the chemicals and so forth being crippled by this. Most American industrial production has not been affected. At the level of administrative staff and expatriates, we will have to watch in the weeks to come to see whether the impulse to get some of them out increases.
As levels of social anxiety rise, so does the possibility, at least, that the normal work styles of expatriate managers and expatriate planners and high-end office people could be affected. That could make some difference. We haven't seen it yet.

Q. Aside from disruptions to exhibitions, conferences, travel and so on, are you seeing more subtle damage in business and economic relations between the United States and China?

A. The obvious, right-in-your-face problems are the problems of holding a trade show. The Canton trade fair was hurt; the auto show in Shanghai was called off a week early. I was involved in a Chinese-sponsored conference in Beijing and, regrettably, the final decision to postpone came only yesterday. Beyond that, when we get into subtle damage, as distinct and isolated as the travel and hospitality industries are, the impact spreads outward from those sectors. Rooms don't get used, detergents don't get bought, uniforms don't get cleaned: there's an inevitable spinoff. It has a depressing effect on economic activity. We'll see more of that. You can't keep flying empty aircraft. The people who work on those planes won't have jobs anymore.
If the trajectory of this disease goes on in the same manner for a prolonged period and the industrial production workplace is affected, that will be a new and difficult challenge for China, and for some customers who rely on Chinese industrial production for things they need to sell elsewhere. That we don't see yet, though it's not impossible. The biggest thing here is that none of us know the duration or virulence of SARS, and a lot hangs on that.

Q. Assuming that the spread of SARS can be halted soon, how long will it take for the economic and business effects to dissipate?

A. My feeling is that at least for China, the economic effects would recede rather quickly. Obviously, if an airline goes out of business or a hotel goes into bankruptcy, the effects aren't cured immediately. But the broad fundamentals for why China has become an important place for business -- those fundamentals haven't changed at this time, and I don't think they're likely to. By the time these kinds of fundamentals changed because of SARS, SARS would likely have spread globally and many other equations would be up in the air.

Q. Where would you put SARS in a ranking of the most significant problems confronting American-Chinese business relations in recent years?

A. I think it has the potential to be quite high if this goes on for an extended period. Crises in China-U.S. business haven't swept across all businesses before. The biggest crisis was in 1989, during the Tiananmen crackdown, when vast numbers of companies said, "Let's get out of here." But today that seems like something from another era. It almost doesn't apply in this ranking. Even the bombing of China's Belgrade embassy in 1999, or the EP3 spy-plane crisis on Hainan Island in 2001, didn't cause a crisis in business. A long run of this disease, with a lot of social disruption, could cause a more serious business emergency.

HEADLINE: ON THE JOB; 
Goodbye Power Lunch. Hello Brown Bag.

BYLINE:  By LAWRENCE VAN GELDER 

 BODY:
"When the going gets tough, the tough get going." That bit of pep talk has been attributed to both Joseph P. Kennedy and Knute Rockne. Regardless of which of these successful men said it first, there is little doubt that hard times lead to hard decisions, and right now more than a few people have been figuring out ways to trim their personal budgets.
 "The world I live in is all about being hip and keeping up with the trends," writes Cornelia Frame of Los Angeles, who works in Hollywood as an account executive in an entertainment marketing department. 
 "Since my job is freelance, and they aren't ready to hire me because of recent financial cutbacks, I have to be really cost-conscious," she writes. 
"But looking great is really important here," says Ms. Frame, who is originally from the Upper West Side of Manhattan.
So how has she addressed the problem?
"I have been coloring my own hair, bringing lunch and borrowing clothes from friends when I get the urge to go shopping," she says. "I learned to give myself a manicure every week, cut out the maid at home" and converted American Express Membership Rewards points into credit at Saks Fifth Avenue so she could go on a shopping spree. "I may even change cellphone plans to get a deal," she added, "and cut out HBO at home.
"It's better to read anyway, right?"
Rising prices at the fuel pump have led Larry Sternbane of Tempe, Ariz., to ponder not only cost-cutting travel but also the impact of gasoline prices on urban development, mass transportation and the location of housing and workplace.
"When the price of gas goes up, some of us don't feel it," Mr. Sternbane writes. "It is possible, even in cities without great mass transit systems, to choose one's home and workplace so that transportation by car is but an option."
Mr. Sternbane goes on to say: "My roommate, Adam Huang, is a graduate student in computer science at Arizona State University; I am a part-time student in Chinese there and a supervisor in the flight kitchen at Phoenix Sky Harbor International Airport. We choose to live in between the two.
"Adam bikes to school most days, or takes the free neighborhood circulator bus directly to campus when the weather is inclement. I take the bus to work most days; once a week I drive in, so that I can go food shopping on the way home. On days when I have school, I bike to school. Then the bike and I hop a bus to get to work. A tank of gas lasts me two months.
"Let the price rise to $5 a gallon. Those who choose to live far from work, school or shopping will feel this burden most. Put the revenue toward increasing mass transit options. There should be a severe economic disincentive to urban sprawl. This approach should do it." 
P. Andrew Payton of Augusta, Ga., used to be a machinist in a factory in South Bend, Ind., that manufactured parts for John Deere.
He writes: "Now that I have to work part time in retail as a supermarket grocery clerk, I save money by walking to work (three blocks away), brown-bag my lunch, don't buy anything from the deli and hand-wash any parts of my uniform that I can. I hang it to dry instead of using the Laundromat every day.
"It comes out just as fresh and clean anyway."
For Jim Anderson, cost-cutting begins at home in Los Angeles, which also happens to be where he works as a manufacturer's representative in factory automation equipment and lives with his wife, Lynn. He compiled a list of cost-cutting measures after poring over expenses for clothing, entertainment, meals and household and business expenses. The list includes steps like these:
* Raising deductibles on auto and health insurance.
* Brown-bagging lunches when possible.
* Washing their own cars (twice monthly).
* Washing and ironing shirts (to avoid dry-cleaning expenses).
* Doing more of their own gardening (reducing the gardener's bill) and cutting down on watering.
* Using public transportation to downtown areas to save on parking costs.
* Washing and brushing the dog instead of sending her to be groomed.
* Reducing maximum driving speed to less than 60 miles an hour, to save gasoline.
* Dining out only once a week (and sharing portions).
* Doing more work by phone when possible, instead of visiting clients personally.
* Going to discounted matinee movies; buying rush tickets to plays for just $10.
Mr. Anderson adds, "Since I run my business from home and shipping costs are part of it, I have begun asking my customers to pick up shipping costs when possible."
