HEADLINE: More Than Money Is at Stake in Votes ByAirline Unions 

BYLINE:  By MICHELINE MAYNARD

 BODY:
Workers in other industries could only dream about the rules of everyday conduct established by agreements between the major airlines and their unions over the last few decades. Pilots worked 80 hours or less during an entire month. Mechanics were paid for waving planes away from gates. Flight attendants got to stay in luxury hotels on the road.
 Over the last two years, though, billions of dollars in losses have forced not just deep pay cuts on airline unions. They also have brought an overhaul of the so-called work rules that will drastically revamp how employees of the major airlines do their jobs. The inspiration is profitable airlines like Southwest and JetBlue, and the goal is survival. 
 But the process of rewriting the rules has brought rancor between unions and management to a new high. Last week, American Airlines' parent, the AMR Corporation, was led to the brink of bankruptcy in part by a fight over flight attendants' schedules. The next test comes today, as flight attendants and machinists at United Airlines, which is bankrupt, vote on a collective $1 billion in concessions sought by the airline, which has asked a court to impose restrictive new contracts on its employees if they do not go along. 
Executives at airlines that have pushed hard to tighten work rules say the changes are a turning point. "What we've got now is a system that's significantly more competitive and frankly makes a lot more sense," said Jerrold A. Glass, the senior vice president for employee relations at US Airways.
Yet, the attack on work rules strikes at the very heart of the airline unions' strength, said Gary Chaison, a professor of industrial relations at Clark University in Worcester, Mass. Their dissolution is viewed within the labor movement as further erosion of the unions' sway. "These are things built over time, and once you go after them, then everything is open," he said.
United's employees are voting on a six-and-a-half-year contract that Glenn F. Tilton, the chief executive of United's parent, the UAL Corporation, called critical to the airline's future. United's pilots have already granted $1.1 billion in givebacks over that same period.
The United mechanics' union, the International Association of Machinists and Aerospace Workers, was the only airline union to reject contract concessions before the airline filed for bankruptcy last December.
A complicating factor this time is an organizing drive by a rival union, the Aircraft Mechanics Fraternal Organization, which has fought concessions at other carriers. Airline officials said yesterday that they were hopeful the contracts would be approved by both unions.
Even before United secures all its concessions, Northwest Airlines is joining the fray. It has told its employees it wants $1 billion a year in concessions, including sweeping work-rule changes, by July 1, and has issued proposals to each of its major unions.
Northwest is not in as bad shape financially as its rivals, but it has watched them successfully, if not harmoniously, roll back work rules. Only last week did Northwest's chief executive, Richard H. Anderson, raise the prospect of a Chapter 11 filing if the airline cannot get the concessions it seeks.
The situation dismayed Mollie Reiley, a veteran of three decades at the airline and trustee for Teamsters Union Local 2000, which represents its flight attendants.
"They're coming to us and asking us to undo 25 years of negotiating," said Ms. Reiley, flipping through Northwest's contract proposal in the back of a taxicab on her way into Manhattan last week to attend the airline's annual shareholders' meeting. "I don't think there's a union in the country that would willingly step up to this."
By far the most significant work-rule change is the elimination of a practice called "receipt and dispatch," in which only licensed mechanics are allowed to wave planes to or from airport gates, even though baggage handlers and other ground workers could easily do the task.
The practice required the airlines to keep hundreds more mechanics on staff than were needed to fix airplanes. It also meant mechanics, some earning $100,000 a year or more, had to be scheduled to work during the day, although repairs are made mostly at night.
US Airways has eliminated the practice at all but 6 major airports; United wants to end it at 18. In court documents, United estimated that it could eliminate almost 900 jobs if other workers could do the task.
Flight attendants at United, US Airways and American also face changes. United, which used to promise flight attendants 17 hours on the job per three-day assignment, now would guarantee only 15 hours. And it is proposing to have crews return from overseas flights after one day's rest instead of two, saving the airline on hotel bills and meals.
American stops paying flight attendants the moment they leave a jet, though they might not reach their homes or hotels for an hour or more. Steven Baumert, an American flight attendant based in Miami, said work-rule changes mean that he must spend more time on the job and that he earns less than he did for the same number of hours under the old contract.
John McCorkle, a flight attendant at US Airways who writes an industry newsletter, said, "They've basically changed every little thing, and all those little things add up to the employee working more days for less pay." 
Pilots, by far the best-paid airline employees, have also faced their share of changes. Northwest's proposal to the Air Line Pilots Association, a union with 66,000 members at 42 airlines, would raise the maximum time that a pilot can fly to 90 hours a month, from 81 hours a month now. Pilots also could end up working more days per month.
Vacation time is another target. Under Northwest's proposal, pilots would have to abandon the practice of signing up for assignments that start before their vacations end, allowing them to stretch their holidays.
Under current work rules, a pilot on a two-week vacation is allowed to bid for a trip that begins the latter part of the second week. Because the vacationing pilot is not available to come to the airport, the airline must call in a reserve pilot, one who is not actively on the job, to take the shift. The pilot who signed up for the assignment gets paid and gets the time off, while the reserve pilot does the work and also gets paid.
US Airways already bans that practice, and its contract eliminated another work rule that sometimes caused flights to be delayed for hours. Under previous contracts, if a pilot called in sick, the airline had to bring in a reserve pilot to replace the ill pilot, even if there were pilots on hand at the airport waiting for flights that were hours away. Passengers simply had to wait until the reserve pilot arrived. Now, the airline is allowed to assign the flight to a waiting pilot, and turn that pilot's later assignment to the reserve pilot when he or she arrives.
Mr. Glass, the US Airways vice president, said the streamlining has only begun. "To be competitive and survive, we have to keep working at improving the systems we have, and we're not there yet," he said.
But Ms. Reiley, the Teamsters trustee, said her members were "getting very angry" and would keep fighting to protect their contracts.

CORRECTION-DATE: May 1, 2003

 CORRECTION:
An article in Business Day on Tuesday about changing work rules in the airline industry referred incompletely to the hours worked by pilots in a month. While contracts with the airlines limit them to about 80 hours in the cockpit, they spend additional time on other duties.

HEADLINE: Congress, Back at Work, Tries to Mend Breach Over Tax Cut 

BYLINE:  By CARL HULSE

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 28 

 BODY:
Congress returned today, and Congressional Republicans quickly set out to devise a strategy to enact President Bush's proposed tax cut and repair a rift between House and Senate leaders over the size of the reduction.
 Congressional aides said the House speaker, J. Dennis Hastert, and the Senate majority leader, Bill Frist, were scheduled to meet Tuesday evening at the White House with President Bush, with the tax package and the spring legislative agenda likely to be major topics of discussion. The full Republican leadership of the House and the Senate is to hear from the president on Wednesday morning. 
 "This will be a discussion about the Congressional agenda," said Bob Stevenson, a spokesman for Dr. Frist, about the Tuesday session. "Obviously the jobs and growth package will figure into that." 
It will be the first meeting between the president and his two chief allies in Congress since a budget deal reached two weeks ago in the Senate deteriorated into acrimony over Dr. Frist's failure to tell his House counterparts about an agreement among some senators to hold tax cuts to $350 billion, rather than $550 billion sought by the House.
Mr. Stevenson said Dr. Frist, who spent much of the two-week recess in Asia on a Senate trip, realized he had slipped up by not informing the House and was trying to contend with the consequences while pushing for the largest possible tax cut number.
"It goes with the turf," said Mr. Stevenson, about the criticism leveled at the majority leader.
Senator Mitch McConnell of Kentucky, the No. 2 Republican in the Senate, said he "absolutely" believed that Dr. Frist would be able to put the issue behind him. After an hourlong private meeting of Senate Republican leaders late this afternoon, Mr. McConnell said he did not think the dispute would present "any lasting problem either within the Senate or across the Capitol," referring to the House.
In the House, officials said the leadership was trying to move beyond the political spat and make some progress on the tax cut package, the chief Republican domestic economic initiative.
"We don't have to have some rehash of what went wrong," said Stuart Roy, a spokesman for the House majority leader, Tom DeLay. Mr. DeLay had said immediately after learning of the Senate side deal that there could be long-term consequences for the House-Senate relationship. Mr. Roy said Mr. DeLay's view now was "that we don't need some big mea culpa meeting to hold hands and sing 'Kumbaya.' " He said the best solution was to get an acceptable tax deal.
House officials also said there was sentiment that Dr. Frist was entitled to some leeway for being so new in his job, which he took over in January after Senator Trent Lott, Republican of Mississippi, was forced to step down.
Senator Charles E. Grassley of Iowa, the Republican who is chairman of the committee that will have to write the tax plan, said today that he expected the House to pass tax cut legislation near the full $550 billion, while the Senate would probably be closer to "$350 billion plus," with any amount over the $350 billion offset by spending cuts or other tax changes.
In an interview on CNN, Mr. Grassley also appeared to issue a warning to Mr. Bush and fellow Republicans that they should tread more lightly when it comes to pressuring some in the party to back a bigger tax cut.
Conservative groups have been running advertisements criticizing two Republican senators, Olympia J. Snowe of Maine and George V. Voinovich of Ohio, for opposing a larger tax cut, and Mr. Bush last week campaigned for the tax cut in Mr. Voinovich's home state.
"If Republicans push too hard, you have what happened to us two years ago, with Senator Jeffords leaving the party," said Mr. Grassley, referring to the party switch by James M. Jeffords of Vermont that cost Republicans control of the Senate in 2001. He quickly added that he knew of no Senate moderate considering a switch but said that Republicans should "learn from history."
As the Senate went back to business, Democrats and Republicans picked up where they had left off, with a floor fight over a judicial nomination. This time the center of the fight was Jeffrey Sutton, selected by Mr. Bush to serve on an appeals court in Cincinnati. Mr. Sutton's nomination is being opposed by Democrats and some advocates for the disabled, who question his record as a lawyer on disability issues. A vote is expected Tuesday.
Dr. Frist, in a speech to a medical group today, did try to restore some momentum for another domestic initiative, the administration's proposal to provide some prescription drug coverage for Medicare beneficiaries.
In a speech to the American Hospital Association meeting, Dr. Frist said the "legislative stars in Washington are aligned" for adding a prescription drug benefit to the health insurance plan for older Americans. He said he expected a Senate vote on a drug plan before the Fourth of July recess, with the House potentially moving more quickly.
But he warned that the window for approving a plan was narrow, with the presidential election campaign becoming a factor after the summer. Democrats say the president's $400 billion plan is not comprehensive enough.

HEADLINE: Finding Fraud on Wall Street 

 BODY:
After five months of negotiating the fine print, 10 Wall Street firms accused of issuing tainted stock research agreed yesterday to the terms of a landmark $1.4 billion settlement. Three of the firms -- Citigroup's Salomon Smith Barney, Merrill Lynch and Credit Suisse First Boston -- were charged with actually issuing "fraudulent" stock recommendations. This unambiguous language, not the fines' dollar amounts, may be the most significant outcome of the inquiry undertaken by the New York attorney general, Eliot Spitzer, and other regulators. 
 The language sets the record clear: brokerage firms compromised their integrity in an effort to curry favor with their banking clients at the expense of individual investors. Along with a treasure trove of new evidence, this finding should strengthen the legal position of investors seeking restitution through private litigation. 
 Jack Grubman and Henry Blodget, analysts who became celebrities during the market bubble, agreed to a lifetime ban from the securities industry. They will also pay fines totaling $19 million, a modest sum in light of their combined compensation and the nature of their behavior. But as with their former employers -- Citigroup and Merrill Lynch -- these fines may represent only a fraction of their eventual liability. 
Mr. Spitzer's inquiry became front-page news when he produced e-mail showing how Mr. Blodget, Merrill's Internet analyst, touted stocks he privately called "junk." Mr. Grubman issued similarly inflated valuations to please Citigroup's investment banking clients. Evidence released yesterday also pointed to improper contacts between Mr. Grubman and Sanford Weill, Citigroup's chairman. Citigroup is paying the largest penalty and is being singled out for the most intrusive monitoring of its research operations.
The settlement's structural reforms, announced last December, are aimed at insulating research analysts from their investment-banking brethren. Brokerage firms must also provide clients with competing independent research and end the infamous practice, called "spinning," of allotting hot initial public offering shares to favored clients. Firms are already adopting many of these reforms to regain investors' trust. Even experienced investors were shocked to discover how widespread these practices were. The settlement should be closely monitored to prevent their reappearance.

HEADLINE: WALL STREET SETTLEMENT: THE ATTORNEY GENERAL; 
Finding Fraud On Wall St. May Be a Step To Higher Post

BYLINE:  By RAYMOND HERNANDEZ

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 28 

 BODY:
The question was about tax loopholes and whether the 10 securities firms that agreed to pay $1.4 billion to resolve charges of wrongdoing by their research analysts would be able to squirm out of their plight by writing off their fines. It put the men behind the lectern at the Securities and Exchange Commission's headquarters on the spot.
 But not Eliot Spitzer. "Maybe I can be a little less discreet," Mr. Spitzer, the attorney general from New York, chimed in. "I always try to be." 
 With that, he shifted the focus to Congress, urging lawmakers to act to close the loopholes, and insisting his office and the S.E.C. had done their jobs.
"Spoken like a man whose Hill is in Albany and not in Washington," said William H. Donaldson, the chairman of the S.E.C., to laughter. 
Mr. Spitzer's hill, some say, is just about anywhere he wants it to be these days, having been indiscreet enough to take on the biggest names on Wall Street during a relentless investigation of securities fraud. For more than a year, he has plunged forward, making cases and headlines along the way, and some say paving his future with stepping stones bearing the names of Henry Blodget, Jack B. Grubman and the like.
At the news conference here today announcing the settlement of the case against the 10 firms, Mr. Spitzer was just one player of many. Nonetheless, it was a defining moment for Mr. Spitzer, who just five years ago took a gamble and used his family's vast wealth to oust Dennis C. Vacco, the Republican attorney general at the time.
As attorney general, Mr. Spitzer revived the long-dormant Martin Act, a 1921 state law giving the attorney general of New York jurisdiction over securities trading.
Mr. Spitzer uncovered, among other things, damaging e-mail messages among stock analysts at Merrill Lynch & Company, the nation's biggest brokerage firm and a mainstay of New York's financial community. He accused the analysts of urging customers to buy stocks that the analysts believed were losing bets just so that Merrill could curry favor with companies it wanted as clients.
In doing the job, Mr. Spitzer, 43, has followed in the tradition of activist and consumer-oriented attorneys general, like Louis J. Lefkowitz, who set the standard, and Robert Abrams.
But in his prepared remarks, he likened the pursuit of Wall Street evildoers to another crusader, Theodore Roosevelt, the populist Republican president who dubbed himself a Trust Buster and crusaded against unchecked corporate wealth and power.
"We are at a rare moment," he said. "It is akin to the moment we were at 100 years ago."
Before he was president, Mr. Roosevelt was New York's governor, and many say that is the real goal of Mr. Spitzer.
Indeed, many of those who make their living from Wall Street are seething over Mr. Spitzer, blaming him for pursuing scandal at the expense of their livelihoods and New York's economy. Many complain, quietly, of course, that Mr. Spitzer has relentlessly undermined the public's trust in the stock market while boosting his own political fortunes.
Many of Mr. Spitzer's fellow Democrats, however, feel otherwise.
"New York Democrats need someone who can galvanize them," said Hank Sheinkopf, a Democratic consultant in New York who was one of Mr. Spitzer's campaign advisers in 1988. "Eliot Spitzer appears to be that person right now."
 "This is not without its perils," Mr. Sheinkopf added. "The danger is that he has angered a large portion of New York's traditional fund-raising base, Wall Street."
Mr. Spitzer, in an interview after the news conference, acknowledged the potential difficulties. "This case has left me without some friends I had before," he said.
But, he said, it might have made him friends elsewhere. "I've got a job to do and I'm going to do it," he said. "I hate to sound overly moralistic, but it's the only way to do this job."
Mr. Spitzer also said it was premature for him to talk about a possible campaign for governor, though people in both parties say he is the man to beat should he choose to run for governor in 2006. "All I can tell you is that I have made no decision about that," he said.

GRAPHIC: Photo: The New York State attorney general, Eliot Spitzer, holds up the results of an investigation of Wall Street investment companies. (Associated Press)

HEADLINE: Powell, Denouncing Crackdown, Calls Cuba 'Aberration' in West 

BYLINE:  By CHRISTOPHER MARQUIS

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 28 

 BODY:
Secretary of State Colin L. Powell criticized Cuba today as an "aberration in the Western Hemisphere" and said the administration is studying ways to respond to the Havana government's recent crackdown on its critics.
 "We're reviewing all of our policies and our approach toward Cuba in light of what I think is a deteriorating human rights situation," Mr. Powell told reporters after a meeting with the Jordanian foreign minister. 
 In recent weeks, a growing number of longtime advocates of greater engagement with Cuba have voiced dismay at President Fidel Castro's actions, including jailing about 75 government critics and human rights advocates and executing three men who hijacked a ferry to try to reach the United States.
"This is disastrous," said Pamela Falk, a professor of international law who has worked to increase cultural visits to Cuba and expand sales of American grain there. "Castro and the government of Cuba have managed to alienate any allies they had."
Representative Jo Ann Emerson, a Missouri Republican who has been at the forefront of opening Cuba to agricultural sales, said she was "appalled and outraged" at the recent executions and jailings.
"My colleagues in Congress and those in the agricultural community deeply believe that exporting our food is good policy for the Cuban people," Ms. Emerson said. "But unless the Cuban regime reverses course, peaceful political and economic change in Cuba will be impossible to achieve."
The entire board of directors of one pro-engagement organization, the Cuba Policy Group, resigned last week in protest. Brian Alexander, the former executive director of the foundation, said some board members were distressed that some of Cuba's best-known dissidents were included in the roundup.
"Every person who has gone to Cuba in recent months probably now knows a political prisoner," he said. "That takes it to a personal level."
Mr. Castro has defended his actions, saying the executions were necessary to curtail a wave of hijacking. In a speech on Friday, he said it was necessary to imprison dissidents to keep James C. Cason, the chief of the American diplomatic mission in Havana, from stirring up antigovernment protests.

HEADLINE: AFTEREFFECTS: THE PRESIDENT; 
Bush, Visiting Michigan, Promises All Iraqis a Voice in a New Government

BYLINE:  By RICHARD W. STEVENSON

DATELINE: DEARBORN, Mich., April 28 

 BODY:
President Bush said today that he would make good on his promise to bring stability to Iraq, and that Iraqis of all faiths and ethnic backgrounds could be assured of a voice in their new government.
 Speaking to an audience made up mainly of Iraqi immigrants, Mr. Bush gave an upbeat account of progress being made by the United States in restoring water and power supplies, addressing health care needs, distributing food and restoring order to the streets. 
 "Every day, Iraqis are moving toward democracy and embracing the responsibilities of active citizenship," he said. "Every day, life in Iraq improves as coalition troops work to secure unsafe areas and bring food and medical care to those in need." 
Mr. Bush did not make any long-term financial commitment to rebuilding Iraq, referring only to the $2.5 billion Congress has already approved for the initial costs. He also avoided any specific predictions about how long the United States would retain a civilian or military presence in Iraq, though he counseled patience after decades of dictatorship.
Saying Iraq had embarked on a "hard journey," he said the United States would stand by Iraq as it worked toward a representative government and a role as an anchor of regional stability.
"America pledged to rid Iraq of an oppressive regime, and we kept our word," he said to cheers from the crowd. "America now pledges to help Iraqis build a prosperous and peaceful nation, and we will keep our word again."
Mr. Bush was interrupted several times by the exuberant crowd, which chanted, "U.S.A., U.S.A!" While he was recounting the story of an Iraqi immigrant to the United States who said she never believed Saddam Hussein would be gone, a man in the audience yelled, "He's gone!"
"You're right he's gone," Mr. Bush replied. 
He said all the people of Iraq, whether "Sunni or Shia or Kurd or Chaldean or Assyrian or Turkoman or Christian or Jew or Muslim," should have a place as Iraq chooses its own leaders.
"America has no intention of imposing our form of government or culture," he said. "Yet, we will ensure that all Iraqis have a voice in the new government and all citizens have their rights protected."
Mr. Bush is expected to proclaim formally this week that the combat operation in Iraq is over. In the one I-told-you-so moment in his speech, he noted that there were people who had doubted that the United States and its allies would be welcomed as a liberating force in Iraq.
"They were mistaken, and we know why," he said. "The desire for freedom is not the property of one culture, it is the universal hope of human beings in every culture."
In Baghdad today, about 300 Iraqi political figures met at the invitation of the United States and Britain, and agreed to call a conference in a month to begin selecting a postwar government for Iraq.
But in a round-table meeting here before his speech with 17 Iraqi-Americans of diverse faiths and backgrounds, Mr. Bush got a first-hand taste of the difficulties he will face in encouraging cooperation and accommodation among Iraq's religious and ethnic groups.
Asad Khailany, a Sunni Muslim Kurd who is a leader of the Kurdish movement in the United States, suggested to the president that Iraq be divided into three autonomous or semiautonomous regions, each with a government reflecting the ethnic and religious makeup of its people. 
Another participant in the discussion, Tarik Daoud, a Christian who was born in Iraq and who had earlier stressed the need to view Iraqis as one people, began to disagree with Mr. Khailany, but Mr. Bush quickly broke in to bring the argument to an end almost before it had begun.
"We're not going to have a debate on the form of the government," Mr. Bush said. "This debate is going to take place in Iraq."
Later he added, "The political issues, which you're rightly concerned about, become easier to deal with when the human condition is improved in a noticeable way."
Mr. Bush heard mostly praise from the 17 participants in the round-table discussion, many of whom urged the United States to stay in Iraq for as long as it takes to insure stability and a transition to a stable government. Despite widespread criticism from the large Arab-American population in the Detroit area that the administration's immigration policies and its aggressive effort to root out terrorism have infringed on civil liberties, none of the participants raised the issue with Mr. Bush.
Mark Dickow, who was born in Iraq and is now a lawyer in Michigan, said he was looking forward to Mr. Bush's winning another term in the White House. Mr. Bush replied, "Forget that, please," adding, "Let's just make sure Iraq works."
It was Mr. Bush's ninth trip as president to Michigan, a state he lost to Al Gore in 2000. In addition to the round-table discussion and the speech, he also met here with the top executives of the three big automobile manufacturers to seek their help in pushing his tax cut through Congress and to ask their views of how the economy is faring.
The White House did not provide any details of the meeting, but the auto executives said afterward that they supported the president's tax plan, including its call for eliminating the tax on stock dividends.

HEADLINE: Citing Funds, Ballet Tech Cancels Next Season 

BYLINE:  By JENNIFER DUNNING 

 BODY:
Eliot Feld's Ballet Tech company will suspend operations for the 2003-4 season, canceling three engagements at the Joyce Theater, Mr. Feld announced yesterday, saying he had made the decision to avoid a projected deficit of $665,000. The company-affiliated school, which offers free dance training and academic instruction to New York City public schoolchildren, will continue, he said. 
 "The cause is twofold," Mr. Feld said in a statement. "One: the pervasive economic woes have affected us much in the same way as they have others, stalling both earned and unearned income. Two: our principal supporter, the LuEsther Mertz Charitable Trust, has gradually reduced its grants to us from a high in 1994 of $822,290 (24 percent of our expenses) to a promise of $350,000 (8 percent of our expenses) in 2004." 
 A spokesman for the charitable trust declined to comment.
The suspension is the first among major American dance companies as they try to cope with the troubled economy. "There has been a general consolidation within many of the companies," said Andrea Snyder, executive director of Dance/USA, a national service organization. "Companies have shortened seasons and downsized dancers." She said middle-size companies like Mr. Feld's, which has 14 dancers and a budget of $4 million, appeared to be struggling the hardest. 
John Munger, director of research and information at Dance/USA, said earned income and public and private giving to companies like Ballet Tech had not changed greatly from 2000 to 2002. "But my indicators suggest that we may see nasty cutoffs," Mr. Munger said, citing recent cutbacks by the Pew Charitable Trust and the McKnight Foundation and a trend of radical adjustments in state arts financing. 
Mr. Feld said the company had held its own in public and private grants this year with the exception of the Mertz Charitable Trust, the company's principal donor. He said he had cut four dancers from the roster and had made cutbacks in set, lighting and costume design as well as in his own salary, and had reduced the school staff. 
The training program, founded in 1978 in partnership with the city, has an annual budget of $1.8 million and provides dance and, from fourth grade on, academic training to 135 children selected by audition from 450 schools. 
"The Board of Ballet Tech Foundation will try mightily during this next year to formalize a sustainable collaboration, both educational and economic, with our partner of 25 years, the New York City Department of Education," Mr. Feld said in his statement.

HEADLINE: Art Auctions Buffeted by Events; 
In an Uncertain Season, 'Distress Sales' and Thinner Catalogs

BYLINE:  By CAROL VOGEL 

 BODY:
Times could not have been worse for Sotheby's, for Christie's and for Phillips, de Pury & Luxembourg. Just as their experts began trying to persuade sellers to put their precious Monets or Warhols on the auction block in sales beginning next week, it seemed the world was against them.
 What collector would want to sell in a shaky economy with war in the air? To make matters worse, no superrich collectors died this winter, so there were no big estates that an auction house could use as bait to lure other property. 
 "In some ways it was harder than after Sept. 11," said David Norman, director of Sotheby's Impressionist and modern art department in New York. "After Sept. 11 the event had occurred. There was disruption and there was fear. This time we were working against the unknown. We were asking for consignments before a war." 
When times are tough, however, distress sales are often what bolster the market. It is no wonder, then, that paintings, drawings and sculptures are being sold this year by troubled companies like Vivendi, Enron and Idemitsu Kosan, Japan's second-largest oil refiner. There is also property from Galerie Seomi, a Korean art gallery, which experts say needs to raise cash to pay debts from clients who defaulted on payments.
Other sellers this spring include personalities like Peter Brant, the newsprint magnate; Jocelyn Wildenstein, the former wife of the art dealer Alec Wildenstein; and Charles Saatchi, the collector and advertising magnate. There are seasoned risk takers selling, too, like Ronald Lauder, the cosmetics heir, and Francois Pinault, the owner of Christie's. Both men have their auction house loyalties, their focused collecting agendas and their belief that great art will sell in any climate.
Most of the catalogs are thinner than in the past. And fearing leaner times, experts were particularly concerned about keeping estimates reasonable.
"We felt it was wiser to have 31 lots of good quality that were well priced and saleable than 50 lots and have a struggle," said Nicholas Maclean, co-head of Christie's Impressionist and modern art department. The work of young, contemporary artists are noticeably absent from sales this spring; experts said they didn't want to take the risk on some of these artists, whose prices seemed to have peaked recently. But the sales include something for almost every other taste. Looking for classic, Impressionist art? Sotheby's sale on May 6 has several outstanding examples. One, a Renoir portrait, "In the Roses (Madame Leon Clapisson)," from 1882, may well turn out to be the season's most expensive painting.
This painting, of a French gentleman's wife sitting in her garden, has not been seen since 1937, when it was part of an exhibition at the Metropolitan Museum of Art. While Sotheby's will not say anything about the sellers, the catalogue raisonne of the artist says Mrs. Huguette M. Clark, a Manhattan collector, owns the painting. It is expected to fetch $20 million to $30 million.
Sotheby's is also offering two Degas pastels being sold by the Museum of Fine Arts in Boston. One, "Dancer" from 1885-90, depicts a lone dancer on a stage as she is about to take her bow. It is estimated to sell for $9 million to $12 million. Christie's also has an important Degas in its May 7 auction of Impressionist and modern art. The work, "Little Dancer at 14," one of his famous bronzes, is a posthumous 1922 cast of a dancer with a cream-colored grosgrain silk bodice and a tulle-and-gauze tutu. Mr. Pinault is selling it after owning it for four years. He bought it for $12.3 million at Sotheby's in 1999.
That sculpture is not the most expensive work in Christie's sale. A Cezanne self-portrait comes with an estimate of $15 million to $20 million. Still the price is low considering that the Wildenstein Gallery was recently offering it privately for $50 million. Painted in 1895 when the artist was 55, it is a sober portrait conveying a kind of wariness through his use of highly arched eyebrows and a penetrating stare. While Christie's will not confirm who the seller is, experts say it belongs to the Iemitsu Kosan Company, which reported in December that it was reducing its $11.4 billion debt.
Competition this season will not be just among auction houses but among artists as well. Big ticket works by masters like Klein, Giacometti and Rothko will go head-to-head, sometimes in the same evening auction. Both Sotheby's and Christie's have a Giacometti sculpture of five gaunt female figures on a rectangular base. Both were conceived in 1950. The only difference is that Christie's bronze has been painted by the artist while Sotheby's has not. The work at Christie's, which is considered the rarer of the two because of the painted surface, is estimated to sell for $8 million to $12 million. The one at Sotheby's is expected to fetch $8 million to $10 million.
Christie's sale of postwar and contemporary art on May 14 has two Rothkos painted a year apart. The smaller of the two, "Brown and Blacks in Reds" from 1957, is 90 inches by 60 inches. It is from the Seagram Collection that Vivendi inherited when it bought the company two years ago. Now this troubled entertainment conglomerate is selling its assets, and the Rothko is expected to bring $6 million to $8 million.
Mr. Pinault is selling the other Rothko in Christie's sale. "No. 9 (White and Black on Wine)" from 1958 is much bigger, 105 inches by 166 inches. This year Mr. Pinault bought a group of important Rothkos that were on loan to the National Gallery in Washington from Bunny Mellon, the widow of Paul Mellon, whose father, Andrew W. Mellon, helped found the National Gallery of Art in Washington. The reason Mr. Pinault is selling this one, experts say, is that he does not believe it is as good as the ones from Mrs. Mellon. Christie's painting, one of this artist's larger classic abstract compositions, is estimated to sell for $8 million to $12 million.
Mr. Pinault is also selling a rare Yves Klein, "Re2" from 1958, one of his trademark deep blue monochromatic paintings with sponges. The next night Phillips is selling "Re 21," another of his monochromatic blue paintings. Christie's Klein is the smaller of the two, measuring roughly 53 inches by 47 inches, and experts say the composition is more successful. (It is estimated to sell for $3 million to $4 million.) Meanwhile Phillips's work from 1960 is larger, measuring 78 inches by 65 inches, and is expected to bring $6 million to $8 million. It, too, comes with impeccable provenance. The seller is Ronald S. Lauder, chairman of the Museum of Modern Art and a friend of Daniella Luxembourg, Phillips's president. It was for sale last summer at the Gagosian Gallery on Madison Avenue.
Phillips, which has moved its headquarters from West 57th Street to Chelsea and drastically reduced its staff and sales schedule, will not have an auction of Impressionist and modern art this spring. It is having a sale of contemporary art on May 15. About three weeks ago, when experts there learned of the Klein Christie's was selling, Phillips changed the date of its sale from Monday, May 12, to Thursday, May 15, because it expected to have a better chance selling its Klein if its auction was second.
"It wasn't specifically about the Klein," said Michael McGinnis, head of the Phillips contemporary art department. "But yes, we're probably in a better position coming in second."
The Phillips sale, like Sotheby's and Christie's contemporary art auctions, is offering fewer works by emerging artists. "That bubbly moment of young people buying cutting-edge art is over," said Tobias Meyer, director of Sotheby's contemporary art worldwide. "People now want the classics, art that endures." Sotheby's for example, is selling a group of works that are designated from the AG Foundation, named for Agnes Gund, the philanthropist and collector. Among the best of the group is Pollock's "No. 17, 1949," one of the artist's famous drip paintings. This one is expected to bring $5 million to $7 million.
Christie's sale on May 14 is the largest of the three, and it, too, features a more classic selection of artists. Besides the Rothkos and the Klein, it has an outstanding rare 1947 work by Gorky. "Year After Year," an unusually lush abstract painting, was sold at Christie's 10 years ago for $3.8 million. Now it has an asking price of $13 million to $15 million. Since there are almost no Gorky paintings from this date in private hands, and since it is what several serious collectors are looking for, Christie's experts do not seem worried by the price.
"People are still buying," said Brett Gorvey, co-head of Christie's postwar and contemporary art department. "All the same players we saw last season are back. The big question is whether the Koreans will be buyers, and whether the market can absorb all that's up for sale."

HEADLINE: COMMERCIAL REAL ESTATE; 
Improving the Neighborhood at Ohio State

BYLINE:  By TERRY PRISTIN 

DATELINE: COLUMBUS, Ohio, April 23 

 BODY:
After antiwar demonstrations some three decades ago at Ohio State University, many merchants along High Street, the commercial road just east of the sprawling campus, boarded up their windows. The university turned its back on the unsightly street and began expanding in the opposite direction, across the Olentangy River to the west.
 But in recent years, university officials have concluded that the jumble of fast-food restaurants and dilapidated storefronts along the stretch of High Street bordering the campus has made it harder to attract high-caliber students and faculty. More than half of Ohio State's 48,000 students live in the suburbs rather than on or near the campus. 
 So, like many universities these days, Ohio State is trying to improve its neighborhood -- most visibly by transforming a 7.4-acre site on High Street, just south of the law school, into a mixed-use project intended both to attract shoppers and stimulate investment. 
The land, now cleared, was once home to 32 businesses, many of them bars that had struggled for more than a decade after the legal drinking age was raised to 21 from 18. About half a mile to the south is the Short North, a neighborhood known for its galleries and antique stores.
Later this year, Campus Partners for Community Urban Development, a nonprofit group created by the university and the city (but controlled by the university), hopes finally to begin building the $120 million project. To be called South Campus Gateway, it is expected to include five buildings with 250,000 square feet of retail space, 70,000 square feet of offices, 200 apartments and parking for 1,200 cars.
To manage the development of the site and handle retail leasing, Campus Partners recently turned to Jones Lang LaSalle, one of several big real estate companies with divisions specializing in helping universities improve their surroundings, get more value out of their real estate and build student housing.
The company has worked with 16 colleges and universities, including the University of Pennsylvania, Georgia Tech and San Jose State University. It has been involved in developing 3.3 million square feet of space for schools and manages 1.3 million more. 
Other real estate companies, including Trammell Crow and the Staubach Company, have also recognized that university development can be an attractive niche. "You're going to see more and more of it," said Gregory S. Latran, who manages higher education services for Trammell Crow. "It's going to be very important from a competitive standpoint for universities to look at not only what they control within the campus envelope but also what's outside it."
This month Howard University announced that Trammell Crow would be the lead developer in a project to rebuild a rundown area near its campus in Washington, D.C.
Universities like Ohio State that coexist uneasily with low-income neighborhoods have two choices, said Terry D. Foegler, the president of Campus Partners. They can either wall themselves off from the community, or engage in "enlightened self-interest."
In 1994, after a woman in the freshman class was killed half a block from High Street, Ohio State created Campus Partners, not only to improve the shopping district but also to increase home ownership in a poor residential neighborhood to the east of High Street, Weiland Park. 
A number of universities have set up nonprofit entities to handle their real estate transactions, but Mr. Foegler argues that his organization is unusual because it is devoted to neighborhood revitalization rather than university expansion.
In addition to the Gateway project, Campus Partners is working with the Ohio Capital Corporation for Housing to acquire and rehabilitate more than 1,300 substandard homes in the area. It started a modest program to encourage faculty members to move to the neighborhood by giving them $3,000 no-interest loans that will be forgiven over five years. It is also trying to create a business improvement district for High Street.
Operating semi-independently has helped defuse some of the suspicion that commonly surrounds town-gown relationships. Wayne Garland, president of Buckeye Real Estate, which owns or manages 1,100 apartments near the school, said Campus Partners' approach was more inclusive than the university's. "They are trying to involve all the stakeholders and get feedback from everybody," he said. "I personally would not have given them the same amount of support if I thought the university was making all the decisions."
Campus Partners qualified for the New Markets Tax Credit program initiated in the Clinton administration to stimulate economic development in low-income neighborhoods. In March, Campus Partners was awarded $35 million in tax-credit allocations that will allow investors a 39 percent tax credit. The money will be used to finance Gateway's retail development, Mr. Foegler said.
The $20 million to acquire the land for Gateway came from the university endowment fund. The city of Columbus has agreed to spend $5.7 million to bury power lines and make other improvements, and the state will pay $4.5 million toward the cost of the parking structure. Most of the remaining money will come from bonds. The university also contributed $5 million toward the purchase of other real estate on High Street.
The Druker Company of Boston was initially selected as the developer and as an equity partner in the project. But last year, Campus Partners decided that it no longer needed an equity partner because the venture's risk had been substantially reduced. A Barnes & Noble bookstore had signed a 20-year lease for 50,000 square feet of space at Gateway, the office space would be leased by the university, and about 70 percent of the housing was to be set aside for law students.
"We were bringing a lot to the table," Mr. Foegler said. Ronald M. Druker said that since his family-owned development company did not handle development on a fee basis, "we felt they would be best served by bringing in someone else."
In January, Jones Lang LaSalle was brought in to replace Druker. Herman E. Bulls, chief executive of the company's public institutions group, said it did not invest in university real estate ventures but operated as a developer on a fee basis. Private and institutional developers often make unsuitable equity partners, Mr. Bulls said. "What happens is that the private developer is looking for an exit strategy," he said, "while the institutional developer wants to be there forever."
An equity developer would also demand a higher rate of return than a university, making it likely that most of the retail space would be leased to national chain stores.
Many in Columbus fear that chain stores will predominate at Gateway. But Robert Welanetz, a consultant who is on Gateway's leasing team, said the goal was to have a mix of local, regional and national retailers, with the national chains occupying no more than 35 percent of the space.
Campus Partners has been criticized for its slow pace, and Mr. Foegler said he spent his first 18 months on the job working with dozens of community groups to gain their support. The task of assembling the land took two years, requiring legal action against some owners that was settled out of court.
Thomas L. Lussenhop, president and chief executive of Columbus Downtown Development, a nonprofit group, was the managing director of real estate for the University of Pennsylvania in the 1990's when it built Sansom Common in West Philadelphia, a site that Penn had owned for years. It included a 55,000-square-foot bookstore, a 238-room hotel and 100,000 square feet of retail, restaurant and entertainment space. Mr. Lussenhop said that the careful approach of Campus Partners would pay off.
"The fact that it's taking a long time," he said, "is merely reflective of the fact that they took on a hard problem. If it were easy to do, the market would have done it."

(Jack Kustron/PhotoJ.com, for The New York Times) Map of Ohio shows the location of Columbus: South Campus Gateway will be developed in Columbus, Ohio.

HEADLINE: THE SARS EPIDEMIC: MARKETS; 
Stocks Rise in Asia on Hopes That Worst of SARS Is Over

BYLINE:  By KEITH BRADSHER 

DATELINE: HONG KONG, April 29 

 BODY:
Stock markets rose sharply across most of East Asia today as investors gained hope that an outbreak of a deadly respiratory disease could be brought under control.
 The advances today, led by airline shares, came after a World Health Organization official said that the risk to travelers from SARS, severe acute respiratory syndrome, was overestimated. 
 The W.H.O. announced Monday that the disease appeared to have peaked and gone into decline in Canada, Singapore and here, and had disappeared in Vietnam, although it is still spreading in mainland China and Taiwan.
The Hang Seng index here leaped 3.6 percent today on the heaviest trading volume in eight months. The Singapore market rose 3.5 percent; South Korea's broad market soared 5 percent; the ASX 200 index in Australia increased 1.2 percent; and the Jakarta market climbed 3 percent. 
Even the stock market in Taiwan climbed 1.4 percent, after three days of heavy losses and despite the quarantine today of a second hospital after 17 workers were suspected of having contracted SARS.
In the currency markets, the New Taiwan dollar, the Japanese yen and many other Asian currencies strengthened against the United States dollar today; Japanese financial markets were closed for a holiday, however.
The exception today was the Shanghai stock market, which dropped 1.8 percent, but this was just half of its gains on Monday. 
The China Securities Regulatory Commission announced this evening the closing of stock markets and futures markets in mainland China until May 12.
While the commission did not explain its action, exchange officials in Shanghai had been predicting that such a move would be made in an effort to control the spread of SARS at once-crowded brokerage firms and trading halls.
At the end of the morning session today at the Hong Kong Exchange, traders took off their bright red jackets and headed for lunch after the market's first big gains since the local government announced an outbreak of a mysterious respiratory disease nearly seven weeks ago.
Donna Tsang, a trader wearing a soft pink face mask that matched her blouse and skirt, said that the SARS problem appeared to be ebbing somewhat. "SARS is not over, it has just calmed down," she said, adding that recent gains on Wall Street had played little role in today's advance.
The exchange urges floor traders to wear face masks and hands them out free, but half the traders were not wearing them today. Masks are also becoming less common on the streets in Hong Kong, the city hardest hit by the disease. There were 12 more deaths from SARS here today, raising the total to 150. 
But there were just 15 new cases of SARS here today, while 49 people were discharged from hospitals after recovering from the disease. Dr. Margaret Chan, Hong Kong's health director, warned this afternoon that people should not let down their guard yet.
"We need to maintain our vigilance," she said at a news conference. 
William C. Belchere, an economist with J. P. Morgan Chase, said that many participants in financial markets remained concerned about the continued spread of the disease in mainland China. Nine more deaths were reported there today, raising the total to 148, along with 202 new cases.
But given that SARS has been present in China since November, there should be hundreds of thousands of cases by now if the disease spread easily, instead of the 3,303 cases reported over the last five months, Mr. Belchere said.
"It looks like the worst of SARS is by us, and that gets people back to fundamentals and the balance sheets of companies," he said. 
Even with today's rally, Asian markets have substantially underperformed markets elsewhere, especially Wall Street. Fears of SARS have sharply curtailed demand in many regional economies, even though the limited number of actual illnesses has meant little disruption in the supply of goods and services.
Many economists are still reducing their forecasts for China's economic output this year as tourism, both domestic and foreign, has collapsed; retail sales have weakened; and the sales of many service businesses, from restaurants to hotels, have slowed. While China's output for the full year is expected to increase as much as 6 percent or 7 percent, the Chinese economy is now widely forecast to shrink somewhat in the current quarter and show slow growth in the next quarter even if the disease is brought under control fairly soon.
But stock markets had been weak across much of East Asia through March and much of April on fears that SARS would be even worse. There had been worries that the disease would infect millions of people within months, as was the case with some of the flu pandemics of the 20th century, and that a widespread outbreak would cause even greater economic disruption than is now seen.
Some floor traders here said that for all the ebullience today, a considerable risk remained both to financial markets and to people as long as the disease remained out of control in China. 
"I'm surprised that the market went up," said Shum Yan-yee, leaving the trading floor today. "SARS may not be over very soon."

GRAPHIC: Photo: At the Hong Kong stock exchange yesterday, many traders continued to wear masks to protect themselves from SARS. (Justin Guariglia/Contact Press Images, for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: First, the War; Now, Investor Consequences 

BYLINE:  By DANIEL ALTMAN 

 BODY:
The nation's first military foray under its new doctrine of pre-emption ended in victory, but the war and the uncertainty it created still cost the economy dearly. Those consequences, along with the potential for more diplomatic and military conflicts, are giving some international investors pause as they decide where in the world to put their capital.
 "The American administration has made it clear that they wish to take that battle to more than just Iraq," said Martin M. Gagen, chief executive of the 3i Corporation for the United States and Asia. 3i is Europe's biggest venture capital firm. 
 "My working assumption is that there will be other events," he said. "It may not be as significant as invading another country, but you could see very high-level diplomatic initiatives against these countries overseas. You'd be foolish to think it would have no impact on the economics." 
In the eyes of many investors, the United States' fundamental economic strength continues to justify its place as a favored target for capital. They still see the flexibility of the nation's markets and its prospects for growth as superior to those of Europe and Japan, and they cite these factors as the most important for investment. But by influencing the economy, the federal budget and international relations, American foreign policy has begun to creep into the picture.
"America is more dependent on the rest of the world for capital than at any time in the past 50 years," said David P. Bowers, chief global investment strategist of Merrill Lynch. "What the rest of the world is being asked to fund is very different from what they were being asked to fund three years ago. Three years ago they were being asked to fund a private sector miracle. Now they're being asked to fund Bush's tax cuts and the war on Iraq."
Mr. Bowers, speaking from London, asserted that America's foreign policy had created tension and vulnerability in its economic situation. "At the end of the day, the U.S. is prepared to act unilaterally," he said. "The bottom line is that if you are a net debtor to the rest of the world, ultimately you have to be multilateral."
In an article he wrote two weeks ago, Mr. Bowers cited "a geopolitical change under way that could spell trouble for the funding of the U.S. economy" as the "most disturbing" risk threatening a strong recovery.
For some other strategists, the nation's nascent unilateralism has become part of a bigger discussion of how investors perceive the American investment environment.
"The current differences of opinion over first the necessity of a war in Iraq, secondly the conduct of the peace in Iraq, and the postwar environment -- they're important yet incidental to the larger question of whether the United States is headed in some longer-term sense on the right course," said Stuart A. Schweitzer, a strategist at J. P. Morgan Fleming Asset Management.
Mr. Schweitzer expressed confidence in the nation's potential for growth, but he said that "heightened tensions with traditional allies" were affecting international investors' confidence in the United States. Mr. Gagen said bad blood had already made it harder to seal big transactions between French and American companies. He said he also worried that the nation's foreign policy could make settlement of trade disputes more difficult.
"The next time there's a major trade negotiation between any of these parties," he said, "we just don't know what sort of impact these things will cause."
Though these frictions are hard to measure, the White House's foreign policy choices have had some tangible effects in the short term. Economists generally agree that the war in Iraq exacted a heavy toll on the economy, even before the fighting began. According to some Wall Street experts, the uncertainty preceding the conflict may have cost the economy $50 billion or more in lost spending by consumers and businesses. Some investors have also worried that the nation's activist foreign policy could hurt businesses that rely on exports.
The direct costs of the war in Iraq to the government are also easily measured. Congress allotted $79 billion to pay for the war and related expenses this year. In addition, the reduction in economic activity that preceded the fighting is likely to have lowered federal tax receipts.
That weakening of the government's fiscal balance is contributing to the concerns of investors here and abroad, Mr. Schweitzer said. "Foreign investors care not only about whether the U.S. is fundamentally right or not about what it's doing in other countries. Foreign investors also attach a great deal of significance to the budget position -- to whether the U.S. is being fiscally prudent or not."
Steve Alexander, a managing director at BNP Paribas who heads the private capital group for North America, explained that geopolitical risks were implicitly "baked into" most indexes of economic activity that investors would watch routinely. "The geopolitical elements probably add some flavor, but I don't think they drive it," he said.
Indeed, some investors consider themselves virtually immune to geopolitical risks. "Our companies are going to come to market in years, not months, so we don't tend to have a short-term economic horizon," said Peter Schwartz, a venture partner at Alta Partners in San Francisco. "You would say that about the people investing with us as well."
But Mr. Bowers contended that if the rest of the world changed its view of the United States, it would cut to the core of the economy. The nation's gross saving rate -- including all sources of saving, from households to governments to corporate accounts -- is at its lowest level since 1945. So increases in investment are likely to depend on foreign funds. "There are some really fundamental issues here which really do matter," Mr. Bowers said.
Not everyone is convinced, however, that a more unilateralist foreign policy is a bad thing for the United States, from an economic perspective. Joseph R. Schmuckler, president and chief operating officer of Nomura Securities International, suggested that the war in Iraq might actually help the United States to attract investment.
"Lack of United States and allied resolve in combating the war on terrorism, and allowing the uncertainties and risks to continue to weigh on investors' minds, is not only a negative for the United States but for other free-market economies around the world," he said. "Without question, doing nothing is not an answer. We can't survive in the long term without fighting back this enemy."

HEADLINE: THE MARKETS: STOCKS & BONDS; 
Good Earnings Reports Propel Shares Higher for 2nd Day

BYLINE:  By The Associated Press 

 BODY:
A succession of better-than-expected earnings reports sent stocks climbing for a second day yesterday as investors grew more convinced that the worst was over for the economy and Wall Street. A surge in consumer confidence fed the buying momentum, and allowed the market to overcome several waves of profit taking.
 Upbeat profit reports again flowed in from big companies, this time, Northrop Grumman, DuPont and Bristol-Myers Squibb. 
 "We basically are on a bit more solid footing than we have been in quite a while," said Steven Goldman, chief market strategist at Weeden & Company in Greenwich, Conn.
And the uptick in consumer sentiment is "a good reason for people to be enthusiastic," said Brian Bruce, director for global investments at PanAgora Asset Management in Boston.
The Dow Jones industrial average closed up 31.38 points, or 0.4 percent, at 8,502.99. On Monday, the Dow climbed 165.26 points.
The broader market also advanced. The Nasdaq composite index rose 9.06 points, or 0.6 percent, to 1,471.30. The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index advanced 3 points, or 0.3 percent, to 917.84.
Investors seemed torn between caution, which manifested itself in profit taking, and a desire to be part of the market's latest advance.
"After the rally we have had," said Brian Pears, head equity trader at Victory Capital Management in Cleveland, "people have become a lot more selective about their purchases. The cliche is, 'The easy money has been made at this point.' " 
Economic news was encouraging. The Conference Board reported that consumer confidence rose sharply in April after the swift outcome in Iraq. 
Investors pay close attention to the mood of consumers, whose spending accounts for two-thirds of the economy.
There was also good news about workers' wages and benefits, which the Labor Department said rose by 1.3 percent in the first quarter. It was the biggest increase in nearly 13 years and almost double the 0.7 percent gain seen in the fourth quarter of 2002.
Over the past few weeks, companies have reported surprisingly strong profits and economic readings have turned more positive, prompting investors to buy stocks with renewed confidence.
"Investors were bracing for a slowdown in the economy," Mr. Goldman said. "If anything, earnings are coming in a tad bit better than expected and that is alleviating some uncertainty."
Among the gainers, Northrop Grumman climbed $2.76, to $89.29, after posting first-quarter earnings that were 35 cents a share higher than Wall Street expected and increasing its 2003 outlook.
DuPont rose 64 cents, to $42.37, on quarterly profits that surpassed analysts' expectations by 7 cents a share. Bristol-Myers Squibb advanced 85 cents, to $25.21, after reporting earnings that were a penny a share higher than analysts anticipated.
But Alliance Capital fell 85 cents, to $31.50, on first-quarter earnings that missed analysts' expectations by 5 cents a share.
Treasury bond prices fell yesterday as stocks gained. The benchmark 10-year note lost 6/32, to 99 19/32. The note's yield, which moves in the opposite direction from the price, rose to 3.93 percent from 3.90 percent on Monday.
The 30-year bond fell 7/32, to 108 5/32. Its yield climbed to 4.84 percent from 4.83 percent.

HEADLINE: Consumer Confidence Shows a SubstantialGain 

BYLINE:  Bloomberg News 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 29 

 BODY:
Consumers' confidence in the economy jumped this month by the most in 12 years, a private research group said today, as the fighting in Iraq wound down, energy costs fell and stock prices rose.
 The group, the Conference Board, said its consumer confidence index increased to 81 from a reading of 61.4 in March. The rise was the biggest since March 1991, the month in which the previous war with Iraq ended. 
 "One barrier to growth has tumbled and that opens the door to brisker household spending," said James O'Sullivan, an economist at UBS Warburg in Stamford, Conn. A sustained rise in consumer confidence is needed to generate more spending for an economy that has grown at an annual rate of less than 2 percent in each of the last two quarters, economists said.
A separate report from the Labor Department showed that first-quarter labor costs rose the most in 14 years as employers paid more for benefits. The employment cost index increased 1.3 percent, almost double the fourth-quarter rise of 0.7 percent. Benefit costs surged 2.2 percent, reflecting higher expenses for health insurance and retirement benefits.
For the confidence index, analysts had forecast a level of 70 this month after a previously reported March reading of 62.5, based on the median of 62 forecasts in a Bloomberg News survey. The March reading was the lowest since October 1993.
Rudolf Thunberg, an economist at Reid Thunberg & Company whose forecast of 80 was the highest in the survey, said he doubted that sentiment would keep rising. As the previous war with Iraq showed, he said, "the postwar euphoria tends to fade and the weak labor market is likely to keep consumers cautious." About a half-million jobs were lost in the last two months.
Consumers' assessment of the current job market edged up this month from a nine-year low. The percentage of consumers who saw jobs as plentiful rose to 13 percent compared with 11.4 percent in each of the previous two months. The percentage that thought jobs were scarce decreased to 29.5 percent from 32.3 percent.
The part of the confidence index that tracks consumers' expectations for the next six months rose to 84.8 from 61.4, also the biggest gain since March 1991. A gauge of optimism about the present situation rose to 75.3 this month from 61.4 in March, the biggest gain in more than a year.
A fall in oil prices and a stock rally may be leading to optimism. In the last two months, crude oil prices have fallen by a third since reaching a 12-year high of $39.99 a barrel on Feb. 27 in the buildup to the war. The Standard & Poor's index of 500 stocks is up 14 percent since reaching a five-month low on March 11.
The Conference Board surveys 5,000 households about general economic conditions, their employment prospects and their spending plans. The survey reflected responses received through April 22.

HEADLINE: Report Finds Deep Poverty Is on the Rise 

BYLINE:  By SAM DILLON 

 BODY:
The number of black Americans under 18 years old who live in extreme poverty has risen sharply since 2000 and is now at its highest level since the government began collecting such figures in 1980, according to a study by the Children's Defense Fund, a child welfare advocacy group.
 In 2001, the last year for which government figures are available, nearly one million black children were living in families with after-tax incomes that were less than half the amount used to define poverty, said the new study, which was based on Census Bureau statistics and is to be released publicly today. The defense fund provided a copy in advance to The New York Times. 
 The poverty line for a family of three was about $14,100, the study said, so a family of three living in extreme poverty had a disposable income of about $7,060, the study said. 
 In early 2000, only 686,000 black children were that poor, the study said, indicating that the economic circumstances of the United States' poorest black families deteriorated sharply from 2000 to 2001.
 Deborah Weinstein, the director of the division of the Children's Defense Fund, who oversaw the research that produced the study, said its release had been timed to influence the national debate over President Bush's tax cut proposal, which her group opposes, as well as deliberations in the Senate, where the 1996 law that reshaped the nation's welfare landscape is up for reauthorization. 
 The Children's Defense Fund has been a consistent critic of the vast overhaul of the American welfare system carried out during the 1990's.
 "The study shows that in the first recession since the welfare law took effect, black children who have the fewest protections are falling into extreme poverty in record numbers," Ms. Weinstein said. "So as we consider our federal policies, are we going to help children who need help the most, or rich people who don't need help at all?"
 Supporters of the welfare changes of the past decade characterized the study as an effort to focus on a narrow slice of bad news, while ignoring what the supporters see as the overwhelming benefits that the overhaul had for most poor families.
 "The Children's Defense Fund searched with a laser for something that was negative to say, because the poverty picture in America since the 1996 welfare reform is unambiguously positive," said Jason Turner, a visiting fellow at the Heritage Foundation, who as New York City's commissioner of human resources from 1998 to 2001 was in charge of Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani's welfare policies.
 The study focused not just on poor black children, but specifically on black children living in extreme poverty.
 Mr. Turner said that such a study ignored the gains made in recent years by the larger population of poor black children. In 1995, he said, 41.5 percent of black children lived below the poverty line, but by 2001, only 30 percent were living in poverty.
 The generalized decline in poverty among black children was not in dispute.
 "Recent studies show overall poverty has declined among black children, but fail to show the record-breaking increase in extreme poverty among these children," the Children's Defense Fund said in a statement that accompanied the study. "Today's analysis further shows that safety nets for the worst-off families are being eroded by Bush administration policies that cause fewer extremely poor children of all races to receive cash and in-kind assistance."
 Margy Waller, a visiting fellow at the Brookings Institution who was a senior adviser to President Bill Clinton for welfare issues, said that other recent research, including her own, hadsupported the conclusion that the gradual disappearance of safety net programs had driven some of the country's poorest families deeper into poverty, even as the status of other poor families improved. Mr. Clinton signed the 1996 overhaul of the welfare system into law.
 "This data is not surprising to me because other work I've seen has shown that since the welfare reform there have been some increases in extreme poverty, resulting from lost public benefits," Ms. Waller said. "I think that the '96 welfare reform law has been beneficial for many families. But we also know that some families are worse off."

HEADLINE: AFTEREFFECTS: ANTIQUITIES; 
Campaign Starts to Help Iraq Rebuild Cultural Institutions

BYLINE:  By MARTIN GOTTLIEB 

DATELINE: LONDON, April 29 

 BODY:
Representatives of many of the world's great museums gathered amid the ancient artifacts of the British Museum today to announce a campaign to rebuild as much as possible the plundered cultural institutions of Iraq.
 Experts from the Louvre in Paris, New York City's Metropolitan Museum of Art, Berlin's State Museum and Russia's Hermitage unveiled a plan to inventory within six months the losses in a wave of looting at the National Museum in Baghdad, the National Library and other museums and sites. They also pledged to provide assistance to their beleaguered Iraqi colleagues in restoring damaged antiquities. The meeting included archaeologists from leading Western universities and officials of Unesco and the British government. 
 A Unesco representative announced that Koichiro Matsuura, the organization's director general, would meet with Secretary General Kofi Annan Wednesday to ask him to urge the Security Council to pass a resolution imposing an embargo for a "limited period" on the sale of all Iraqi cultural objects on the international market. 
The measures were proposed to curb the growing trade in illicit antiquities that is believed to have absorbed thousands of objects looted from museums and archaeological sites after the first Persian Gulf war in 1991.
Neil MacGregor, the director of the British Museum, said that his institution would act as a clearinghouse for efforts to help Iraqi specialists find looted objects and restore damaged ones. 
Donny George, the research director of Iraq's National Museum, described 20 extraordinarily important items that are missing from its collection after it was ransacked as American troops moved into the city earlier this month. 
They include the "Warka Vase," a sacred limestone piece decorated in relief, dating from 3100 B.C.; marble statues of Poseidon, Apollo and Eros from the fabled ruin at Hetra; and a headless statue, covered in inscriptions, of King Entemena of Lagash, from 2400 B.C. 
Mr. George said that a significant part of the looting was professionally organized by gangs with glass cutters and sets of keys. But the pillage might have been prevented, he said, with only a slightly more vigorous response from from the American military. "Moving a tank for 50 or 60 meters would have saved the museum," he said.
Although some evidence suggests that people with inside knowledge of the museum were responsible for stealing the more valuable items, Mr. George said he had no information indicating that the culprits were officials connected with his antiquities department or with the government of Saddam Hussein. 
Mr. George also reported that many valuable items were saved because they were moved out of the museum in the weeks before the war.
John Curtis, the curator of the British Museum's Near East Collection, who returned from a brief trip to Baghdad with Dr. George, pointed to the theft of a bronze statue of an Akkadian king, saying it seemed to reveal a coordinated effort. 
"It is so large and heavy that it would have required five or six people to move," he said. 
The group also decided to issue an appeal to the United States "urgently to secure the frontiers of Iraq," Mr. MacGregor said, after hearing of the experience of Mr. George as he traveled to the conference. Mr. George said that he drove from Baghdad to Amman, Jordan, and was first stopped at the border at an American checkpoint. No search was conducted of his belongings, he said. Yet when he arrived at the Jordanian checkpoint he underwent a complete search. He learned that the Jordanians had confiscated artifacts and documents from 12 people during their checks.
Tessa Jowell, Britain's secretary of state for culture, said the government of Prime Minister Tony Blair would seek to toughen Britain's laws on the sale of antiquities and raise additional funds for the project of Mr. MacGregor and his staff.
Sharply questioned about whether Britain had done all it could to ensure that the Americans would protect cultural resources in Baghdad, she argued that nobody could have anticipated the extent of the looters' destruction. 
"Quite clearly what has happened is a disaster," she said.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Tessa Jowell, Britain's secretary of state for culture, and Donny George, the research director of Iraq's National Museum, at the British Museum yesterday. They announced a plan to help Iraq recover treasures. (Agence France-Presse)
