HEADLINE: Republican Leaders of Congress Say They Will End Budget Feud and Strike Deal on Tax Cut 

BYLINE:  By CARL HULSE 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 29 

 BODY:
Republican leaders of Congress said today they were ready to put a budget feud to rest and strike a deal on a tax cut, with the Senate majority leader, Bill Frist, saying his objective now was to get the largest possible tax cut.
 "My goal is to grow that number as high as I possibly can," said Dr. Frist, who this month angered House Republicans by not telling them of a private Senate agreement to hold tax cuts to $350 billion while the House was approving $550 billion. Senator Frist, of Tennessee, said today he had apologized to his counterparts in the House and was ready to move on. 
 "The big mistake, lesson learned, is no surprises," said Dr. Frist, who met in the White House this evening with President Bush and the House speaker, Representative J. Dennis Hastert of Illinois, to map legislative strategy before another White House meeting with the Republican leadership of Congress on Wednesday.
The Congressional budget adopted earlier this month allows up to $1.2 trillion in total tax cuts over the next decade, but the Senate protected just $350 billion of those from procedural obstacles while the House figure was set at $550 billion. The fight remains centered mainly on those numbers.
Dr. Frist said he would push his colleagues to expand the $350 billion by paying for anything above that level through spending reductions and other tax changes, which are referred to as offsets. He said the Senate figure of $350 billion could grow to $450 billion or higher with offsets that he said would be hashed out over the next week.
 "Ultimately, I'd like to get up to $1.2 trillion as we go forward and over the course of the budgeting cycle in terms of appropriate tax policy," he said. White House officials said they would also consider offsets as a way to raise the tax-cut number for what Republicans call a jobs and growth package.
But the offsets will be difficult to achieve in practice. If the money is squeezed out of spending, it would have to come from popular social programs. And some Republicans are already resisting the idea of raising other taxes to pay for new cuts.
 "I think it is counterproductive to raise taxes on one side and lower taxes on another," said the House majority leader, Representative Tom DeLay of Texas.
Mr. DeLay said he now "feels comfortable" that Dr. Frist understands the mistake he made last month and would not repeat it. Mr. DeLay and other Republicans also moved to shift the attention from intramural squabbling to Democratic reservations about the tax cuts.
"We are not going to allow the Democrats to practice the politics of economic destruction again by foul-mouthing the economy at the same time they are trying to submarine the president's economic package," said Mr. DeLay, who said the opposition felt "an obligation to gripe."
 And gripe it did, saying that the Republican tax push was tantamount to a corporation's borrowing money to pay dividends. Most Senate Democrats, joined by a handful of Republicans, oppose any tax cuts that total more than $350 billion.
 "We'll be borrowing money to pay outa tax cut, and a tax cut of a magnitude that will leave us with a debt that is in excess of anything our country has ever experienced in one year," said Senator Tom Daschle of South Dakota, the Democratic leader. He planned a news briefing on Wednesday to highlight his complaints with the Bush administration plan to eliminate taxes on corporate dividends.
With Republicans trying to settle on a strategy and Democrats plotting their response, most of the business on the tax cuts was conducted behind closed doors at locations scattered around the city. Tonight Mr. Bush met with Mr. Hastert and Dr. Frist for an hour and 15 minutes on the Truman Balcony at the White House, where aides said they discussed the tax cut, AIDS policy, Medicare and federal judges. 
 Earlier in the day, Vice President Dick Cheney met at lunch with Republican senators. Then Treasury Secretary John W. Snow and Stephen Friedman, chairman of the National Economic Council, met first with Representative Bill Thomas of California, chairman of the House Ways and Means Committee, and then with Dr. Frist and Senator Charles E. Grassley of Iowa, chairman of the Finance Committee.
Mr. Thomas and Mr. Grassley also met today, then Mr. Grassley conferred with the Republican members of his committee as preliminary steps in determining what the elements of the tax legislation will be. The two chairmen hope to have their committees vote on legislation next week. Under this schedule, bills would be considered on the House and Senate floors using special, expedited rules the following week, and a final agreement could be approved the week before Memorial Day.
 Senator Don Nickles of Oklahoma, chairman of the Budget Committee and a senior Republican on the Finance Committee, suggested today that one way to reduce the 10-year tax-cut number would be to put the reductions in place this year and then end them before the 10-year period was up, employing a process lawmakers call sunsetting.
The theory behind such a practice is that it would be difficult for Congress not to re-enact a tax cut once it was on the books but that the additional money would not count in the 10-year calculations.
Senator Max Baucus of Montana, the senior Democrat on the Finance Committee, said he and other Democrats on his panel were prepared to vote for a tax bill as long as the cost did not exceed $350 billion and there was no reduction in the income tax on stock dividends.

HEADLINE: Hypocrisy & Apple Pie 

BYLINE:  By MAUREEN DOWD; E-mail: liberties@nytimes.com

DATELINE: WASHINGTON 

 BODY:
Richard Perle is at ease with neo-imperial swagger.
 At the White House Correspondents Association dinner on Saturday night, the Pentagon's Prince of Darkness lectured Hans Blix as if he were a colonial subject, instructing him on why an invasion of Iraq had been justified even though no weapons of mass destruction had yet been found. 
 Asked afterward how Mr. Blix had reacted, Mr. Perle replied merrily: "He's a Swedish disarmament lawyer. He's used to a lot of abuse." 
When one partygoer told Mr. Perle that she would miss the buzzy, standing-room-only "black coffee briefings" on Iraq held by hard-liners at the American Enterprise Institute, he suggested the neo-cons might hold another round.
"We'll have green tea briefings on North Korea," he said slyly.
On Fox News, Bill Kristol spoke up for a more brazen imperial attitude. "We need to err on the side of being strong," he said. "And if people want to say we're an imperial power, fine. If three years from now, we have beaten back these threats and have a decent regime there, it'll be worth it."
But imperial flair is rare. America is a furtive empire, afraid to raise its flag or linger too long or even call things by their real names. The U.S. is having a hard time figuring out how to wield its colonial power, how to balance collegiality with coercion, how to savor the fruits of imperialism without acknowledging its imperialist hubris. 
When Kofi Annan called the Americans in Iraq an "occupying power" last week, Bush officials freaked. Maybe they would have preferred Honored Guests.
The Pentagon once more outgunned the State Department this week, changing the name of a new governing body of Iraqis from "interim authority" to "transitional government" to signal that the U.S. would leave quickly and give its Armani-clad puppet, Ahmad Chalabi, an advantage. But it doesn't matter what euphemistic name is used; if there are too many militant Shiite clerics involved, Rummy, the real authority, will tell them to take their camels and vamoose.
"America is the empire that dare not speak its name," Niall Ferguson, the Oxford professor who wrote "Empire," told a crowd at the Council on Foreign Relations here on Monday. He believes that America is so invested in its "creation myth," breaking away from a wicked empire, that Americans will always be self-deceiving -- and even self-defeating -- imperialists.
"The great thing about the American empire is that so many Americans disbelieve in its existence," he said. "Ever since the annexation of Texas and invasion of the Philippines, the U.S. has systematically pursued an imperial policy. 
"It's simply a suspension of disbelief by Americans. They think they're so different that when they have bases in foreign territories, it's not an empire. When they invade sovereign territory, it's not an empire."
Asked in an interview about Viceroy Jay Garner's promise that U.S. military overlords would "leave fairly rapidly," Mr. Ferguson replied: "I'm hoping he's lying. Successful empires must be based on hypocrisy. The Americans can say they're doing things in the name of freedom, liberty and apple pie. But they must build a civil society and revive the economy before they have elections.
"From 1882 until 1922, the British promised the international community 66 times that they would leave Egypt, but they never did. If they leave Iraq to its own devices, the whole thing will blow up."
Afghanistan offers cautionary lessons. It was the abandonment by the U.S. after Afghanistan's war in 1989 with the Soviet Union that stoked the fury of Al Qaeda. The regime of the American puppet Hamid Karzai is still perilously fragile.
As Carlotta Gall wrote in The Times last weekend, after two U.S. soldiers were killed by Afghan rebels: "In a very real sense the war here has not ended. . . . Nearly every day, there are killings, explosions, shootings and targeted attacks on foreign aid workers, Afghan officials and American forces, as well as continuing feuding between warlords."
Exiled Taliban leaders have called for a holy war against the "occupying forces." The religious police are once more harassing and beating women over dress and behavior, and schools that take little girls are being attacked and threatened.
Until we can get democracy stabilized in our new colonies, Mr. Ferguson offers two words of advice: "Better puppets."

HEADLINE: Problems Are Compounded At Indian Point Plants 

BYLINE:  By LISA W. FODERARO

DATELINE: BUCHANAN, N.Y., April 29 

 BODY:
A day after a power failure at the Indian Point nuclear plant complex, officials were dealing with new problems today: an early-morning fire, and criticism of the long hours that security officers there are forced to work.
 The criticism was prompted by a letter to security officers dated April 21 from an official of the Entergy Corporation, which owns the plants. The letter alerted the officers to the likelihood of working "five 12-hour days and occasionally six 12-hour days" to prepare for an antiterrorism drill in early summer. 
 The letter was made available to The New York Times today by the Project on Government Oversight, a national watchdog group in Washington that has focused on nuclear plant security in the wake of Sept. 11. 
Today the Nuclear Regulatory Commission sent out new orders to the nation's nuclear plants concerning security preparations.
Neil A. Sheehan, a spokesman for the commission, said that the agency sent out three orders, including one that limits security officers to working "no more than 48 hours per week during normal operating times." The agency is giving plants 18 months to fully implement the new rules, he said.
Mr. Sheehan would not say whether the commission would let Entergy follow through with its overtime demands.
"There might be enough maneuvering room in this order to allow them to do this, but that's something we're going to have to take a look at," Mr. Sheehan said. "We're very interested in seeing them improve the quality of their security operations. But clearly we don't want to see guards suffering the effects of fatigue."
The fire and power failure occurred within hours of each other and led to the shutdown of both reactors, but were not related to the issue of security, Mr. Sheehan said. The Indian Point 3 reactor stopped operations after a fire started at 2:59 this morning on the main turbine in a nonnuclear area of the plant, he said.
Plant officials say that some insulating material ignited on the outer casing that houses the turbine. Lubricating oil had apparently leaked onto the insulation and that may have caused the fire, but how the oil got there is still under investigation, said Jim Steets, an Entergy spokesman. The fire, which Mr. Steets described as small but stubborn, was extinguished by the plant's fire brigade, without outside help, at 3:50 a.m. Because the fire lasted more than 15 minutes, the plant had to declare it an "unusual event," the lowest of four emergency classifications set by the regulatory commission. As a result, county, state and federal officials were notified in the middle of the night.
On Monday afternoon, the Indian Point 2 reactor shut down automatically when a power failure occurred off site, causing the plant to disconnect from the electrical grid. Mr. Steets said that both reactors were expected to be back in service within a few days. The N.R.C. said it appeared that Entergy had followed the proper guidelines, both in fighting the fire and in immediately notifying the authorities.
But today, Entergy's critics seemed more exercised about the plant's overtime demands than about the fire. The letter to security officers by Fred Dacimo, a site vice president of Entergy who is in charge of Indian Point, also offered them $500 bonuses upon completion of the drill, which will take place over several days this summer.
"Entergy is a company that believes in pay for performance," the letter said. "Upon the successful execution of the force-on-force exercise, I am prepared to give those security officers, who don't call out sick and remain accident-free, a bonus of $500."
Richard L. Brodsky, a state assemblyman who has campaigned to have Indian Point permanently closed, said the bonuses were evidence that the guards were underpaid. "It's bizarre that we're parceling out the security aspects of Indian Point on a bonus basis," he said. "In my opinion, we should not trust Entergy with the security of the plant."
Peter D. H. Stockton, a security expert who was a special assistant to the secretary of energy in the Clinton administration and now works with the Project on Government Oversight, said that more than 20 guards he had spoken with in recent months complained of fatigue, low pay, inadequate training and poor morale.
Mr. Steets of Entergy said the company had hired 40 security officers in the past few months and planned to hire 50 more over the next several months. "It's a significant addition that will help us reduce the overtime burden so that we can be compliant with the orders of the N.R.C.," he said.
Since the terror attacks of Sept. 11, security has been a special concern at Indian Point, which is about 40 miles north of Midtown Manhattan.
Attention has also focused on whether the plant -- particularly Indian Point 2 -- is operating safely. In recent years the reactor has been plagued by minor leaks and safety lapses, including a February 2000 radiation leak that closed the plant for nearly a year.

HEADLINE: THE SARS EPIDEMIC: CANADA; 
ADVICE TO PUT OFF TRIPS TO TORONTO IS LIFTED BY W.H.O.

BYLINE:  By CLIFFORD KRAUSS 

DATELINE: TORONTO, April 29 

 BODY:
Citing an ebb in the SARS epidemic here, the World Health Organization today lifted a week-old advisory urging travelers to delay all nonessential visits to Toronto.
 The decision was welcomed with sighs of relief from Canadian officials, who had lobbied hard for the reversal and disputed the W.H.O. contention that Toronto had been a source of cases outside Canada. They argued that the advisory unnecessarily damaged the Canadian economy and its international image. 
 But the reversal of an advisory declared only last Wednesday raised questions about the W.H.O.'s decision-making process and whether it had given in to intense pressure from a country with enormous sway in the agency's parent organization, the United Nations.
"It erodes their credibility to reverse themselves so suddenly," said Dr. John Last, a professor emeritus of epidemiology at the University of Ottawa. "It is a serious wound to the body politic of public health, to Canada and to the World Health Organization." 
Twenty-one people have died here of SARS, or severe acute respiratory syndrome, and at least five more are in critical condition, making Toronto the most seriously affected locality outside Asia. The W.H.O. linked several cases in the Philippines, Australia and the United States to people who had traveled to Toronto.
But while Toronto still has 39 active SARS cases and 498 people in quarantine, there has not been a new case reported since April 9 in the general community or a new case since April 20 in the hospital system, where the disease has been concentrated. The disease's incubation period is thought to be up to 10 days.
"There is no longer concern of export," said Gro Harlem Brundtland, the W.H.O. director general, explaining the decision. "The magnitude of the probable SARS has decreased."
Dr. Brundtland still maintained that Toronto had exported cases of SARS to other countries, but not for several weeks.
"It was reasonable to issue that travel advice," she said of the original advisory, which covered Toronto, Beijing, Hong Kong and the Chinese provinces of Guangdong and Shanxi. She hinted that the decision to take Toronto off the list could be reversed again if circumstances warranted.
By contrast, much of China remains under the travel advisory. There, SARS caseloads continue to grow, though much more rapidly on the mainland than in Hong Kong. Mainland China reported 389 new cases today; in Hong Kong, the rate of new cases has slowed to about 15 a day, down from 20 to 80 a day earlier in the month.
In all of Canada, there are 148 probable cases of SARS. Four are in British Columbia and the rest are in and around Toronto. All the Toronto cases are linked to a single elderly Chinese immigrant who came home sick from a trip to see her family in Hong Kong in February.
In Washington, Dr. Julie L. Gerberding, director of the federal Centers for Disease Control and Prevention, told a panel of senators today that the United States had identified 52 probable cases, up from 39 last week. Dr. Gerberding said nearly all of the new probable cases involved people who traveled to affected countries, with the exception of one health care worker and one person infected through household contact.
The W.H.O. imposed one condition on the lifting of the travel advisory in Toronto: that Canada step up screening at the airport there for passengers leaving the country. Canada has agreed to employ infrared temperature-taking machines in Toronto and Vancouver to ensure that anyone with a fever is checked before boarding an international flight.
Dr. Last, the University of Ottawa professor, said the World Health Organization should have waited 20 days, or two incubation periods, before reversing the decision, to guarantee that no one carrying the virus, including those who show no symptoms, can pass it on to others.
But Dr. Last's opinion was clearly in the minority here.
"It shouldn't have been done in the first place," said Mayor Mel Lastman, "but I'm glad they've lifted it so we can get back to normal."
Health officials viewed the decision as an endorsement of their efforts, which have centered on identifying SARS cases and isolating everyone who has come in contact with patients.
Over 7,000 people have been put in quarantine in Toronto. Two local hospitals were virtually shut down, and many others delayed elective surgery and other procedures to control contact between medical workers and the public. Three schools were closed, and later reopened.
Most Torontonians seem to have kept their composure through the epidemic, and the W.H.O.'s original decision prompted a new defiance that sent many out to restaurants and shopping malls. People here never took to wearing masks in large numbers outside of hospitals.
But international confidence may take longer to recover before tourists return and commerce recovers.
The Bank of Canada has lowered its 2003 economic growth projections by a full percentage point, and independent economists have said daily economic damage could easily rise to $30 million a day.
Since the advisory was announced, several conventions have canceled visits to Toronto, where 95,000 jobs depend on tourism. Restaurants, hotels and shopping malls have been hit hard by the decrease in tourism.
"We are obviously very pleased," said Len Crispino, head of the Ontario Chamber of Commerce. "Major business investment has been put on hold and it will take time to recover."
The Canadian dollar, which had fallen in recent days, climbed to a three-year high relative to the United States dollar, to 69.36 cents from 69.04, on the news of the W.H.O. reversal.
Canadian health care officials were careful not to gloat and acknowledged their responsibility to protect other countries against a disease that is still spreading in China.
"We know our vigilance must not stop," said Tony Clement, the Ontario health minister. "I want to assure the international community we are not going to let up."
Prime Minister Jean Chretien had personally lobbied Dr. Brundtland, with whom he has had a warm relationship since her days as prime minister of Norway in the 1990's. Other Canadian cabinet ministers, diplomats and health officials have called Geneva and sent a barrage of medical papers and fact sheets to W.H.O. officials and technicians over the last week. W.H.O. officials privately said during the week that they had not previously felt so much political pressure.
Only 10 days ago, the epidemic looked as if it might spread rapidly through the community, as it has in several Chinese cities.
A nurse with SARS had taken two rush-hour trains shortly before she suffered symptoms, setting off a hurried dash by health care officials to find commuters who had traveled in close proximity. A doctor, not knowing he had SARS, had gone to a well-attended funeral.
More than two weeks later, no one has apparently gotten sick from exposure to either one.
But public health experts cautioned that epidemics frequently have their ups and downs, and spillage into the broader community is still possible.
"I need to remind you that Toronto still has an outbreak of SARS," said Dr. Brundtland, "and the lifting of this travel advice does not change the reality that Toronto has a status as an affected area."

HEADLINE: Where Village Meets Green; 
At 150, Park Is a Melting Pot

BYLINE:  By JOSEPH BERGER

 BODY:
It happens every spring.
 New Yorkers by the thousands stream into Central Park to revel in the full-throated glory of the new forsythia and cherry blossoms. There, they are again reminded that the park is the city's common ground, the democratic village green where people of all backgrounds and enthusiasms come to pursue their separate pleasures, for the most part congenially. 
 In a single 843-acre rectangle, there are more or less permanent and overlapping communities of folk dancers, horseback riders, bird watchers, storytellers, dog walkers, nannies, chess fans, volleyball players, softball leagues, joggers, cyclists, tennis players, ice skaters, soccer teams, ultimate Frisbee devotees and pint-size fishermen.
There is a racially polychromed assortment of roller skaters who shimmy to a disco beat around a macadam oval northeast of the Sheep Meadow, and there is a separate set of in-line skaters who slalom through rows of soda bottles. There are petanque devotees who play a French version of the Italian game of boccie on a hardscrabble patch of ground, and there are lawn bowlers, in mandatory whites, who play on a manicured court. There is even a weekly lakeside gathering of fans of a guitar-playing folk singer, David Ippolito. 
"There are so many different villages," said George van der Ploeg, 50, a bird watcher. "It's like New York City, with a Greek section in Queens, Asians in Flushing, the South Americans and Russians in Brooklyn. In a way the park is a mirror of the city itself."
All these neighborhoods coexist in a park that is celebrating its 150th birthday this summer in what many believe is its best shape in at least half a century.
Before Frederick Law Olmsted and Calvert Vaux, the park's designers, came along, large urban parks were virtually nonexistent. Elizabeth Barlow Rogers, Central Park's first official administrator, said large American parks were playgrounds for the aristocracy, for hunting or grazing deer. Olmsted and Vaux wanted a people's park. Even if modern minds might find their rationale patronizing, they laid out footpaths beside the carriage roads so poorer people could use the gentry as a refining influence.
Today, people seem to recognize one another's rights because they essentially share the same reason for coming to the park.
"New Yorkers are busy obsessing about all sorts of things that are terrible, and you get into the park and it lifts you up and makes you over," said Sarah Elliott, a doyenne of the bird watchers. "You come back feeling a lot better."

'The Ultimate Freedom'
 While their origins almost 25 years ago were rather spontaneous, the dance skaters have become so institutionalized that they have an association, publish a newsletter and charge dues of $15 a year. Still, at their oval, lawyers and bike messengers; the rich and the subsidized; black, white, Hispanic and Asian mingle seemingly without effort.
Gyrating hedonistically the other day were a shirtless man in rainbow-colored Ali Baba pants showing off his chiseled chest; a 56-year-old Polish woman, youthful in black Spandex and aviator glasses; a stylish wine importer; and a bearded Asian-studies expert known as Three-Bottle Bob for the tower of water bottles balanced on his skull.
Many of the 300 regulars come together on weekends because they love shimmying while skating to a throbbing beat before a delighted crowd.
"You bring that child back again," said Felix Santiago, 33, an actor. "There's no yesterday, no tomorrow. No one questions what you do, where you live and where you're from. It's the ultimate freedom."
At least three couples, including Gail Brillhart Myrick, a white textile designer, and Steven Myrick, a black technical support analyst, met at the oval and married. The Myricks have since produced another dance skater, Justin, now 6.
"If the rest of the world got along as well as we do here, ethnically and economically, the world I feel would be a better place," Ms. Myrick said.
Over the years, complaints have risen about the amplified sound from those who prefer a peaceful park. Lezly Ziering, the 70-year-old chairman of the Central Park Dance Skaters Association, has gradually found ways to muffle the music.
Now he tells critics, "Ours is a small circle, and the park is for everybody."

Regulars and Guerrillas
Most groups that start haphazardly eventually get the Parks Department's blessing with a dedicated ground and permit. For more than 30 years, the folk dancers have been doing Balkan, Israeli and other circle dances on Sundays on a plaza near Belvedere Castle. The 30-team Broadway Show League, where on Thursday you could have watched "The Lion King" defeat "The Producers" in softball, has been at the Heckscher ball fields for 50 years. The Model Yacht Club, made up mostly of men who still like playing with small boats (although ones that cost more than $1,000), has its own house at Conservatory Water.
The petanque enthusiasts, most of them workers in the city's French restaurants, have no court or permit. But 30 years ago, in a move that would have delighted such famous Central Park guerrillas as Joseph Papp and Fred Lebow, they simply staked out a shaded plot of earth southwest of the Heckscher ball fields, choosing it precisely because it was rutted and offered challenging plays.
The two dozen regulars include a maitre d'hotel of La Grenouille, the captains at Le Perigord and Rene Pujol and the bartender at Pierre au Tunnel. After four hours of afternoon play they usually adjourn to Tout Va Bien for glasses of Ricard.
They play whenever weather allows, lobbing orange-size steel balls toward a small pink ball called the cochonnet. As each ball lands, there are cries of "tres bien!" or "ne touche pas!" Arguments over which ball is nearest are settled with a collapsible antenna used as a measuring stick.
What Jacques Despretz, the group's spokesman, likes about petanque is that it can be played at any age.
"As long as you stand on your two feet and can raise you hand and hold a glass of Ricard, you have no problems," Mr. Despretz said.
But Michael Touchard, who is younger and works as a bartender at Tout Va Bien, says he particularly likes playing in Central Park because it seems a far more welcoming place than, say, the Bois de Boulogne, the park's equivalent in Paris.
"In Central Park you have everybody, from the rich who walk their dogs to average Joes," he said. "You have a guy who is a busboy and a guy who owns four restaurants, and they all play together."

Sightings and Serenity
Central Park is for the birds, the bird watchers might insist. And it would be hard to argue seeing the single-mindedness with which they go about spotting the more than 200 species that pass through Central Park on their way to more exotic ports of call.
On a recent Wednesday morning, 16 bird watchers with dangling binoculars gathered at Fifth Avenue and ambled into the Ramble, one of the world's great migratory way stations. Their leader was Ms. Elliott, a red-crested sparrow of a woman who has been leading paying tours of the park's bird life for 25 years.
She pointed out the scraggly nest of a red-tailed hawk tenderly patched together above a stone decoration on the top floor of a regal Fifth Avenue building. Deeper into the park, the bird watchers spied a red-bellied woodpecker, a red-headed woodpecker, a great egret, a white-throated sparrow, a red-winged blackbird, a cardinal, a downy woodpecker, a barn swallow, a rough-winged swallow and a double-crested cormorant.
"This time of year the males have two wonderful eyebrows," Ms. Elliott said, referring to the cormorants gliding below Belvedere Castle. "They're sort of like Groucho Marx, except their eyebrows are twisted and corkscrewed, and that shows the female they mean business."
It is this kind of patter that keeps bird watchers flocking to Ms. Elliott, a former teacher. Of course, the bird watchers were also drawn by the astonishment of "a sighting," craning their necks to check out the flash of a woodpecker's brilliant red crown on a murky branch or gasping at the anomaly of a great egret in Turtle Pond.
But they were also enjoying the serenity of the woodlands and hidden meadows.
Ms. Elliott says her group is made up of "people of the leisure class": retirees and workers with flexible hours. They are people like Mr. van der Ploeg, a real estate broker, and Marge Benedetto, who worked at Coronet and Esquire magazines before rearing the four children who have made her a grandmother 11 times over.
"There's something about living in a high-rise and then having all this," Mrs. Benedetto said, running her eyes across the park's generous expanse. "I guess we're all similar. We like the outdoors and we enjoy being with Sarah Elliott. She's unique."
Ms. Elliott says the members of her community come in as many varieties as the birds. "Some are very much loners and very solitary," she said. "And some are social. They talk all the time. You think this poor person is in great need of talking, and then you find out what they're talking about. They could be comparing recipes. That's when I put my foot down and say, 'Quiet!' "

HEADLINE: Virginia Law Makes Spam, With Fraud, A Felony 

BYLINE:  By SAUL HANSELL 

 BODY:
In the toughest move to date against unsolicited commercial e-mail, Virginia enacted a law yesterday imposing harsh felony penalties for sending such messages to computer users through deceptive means.
 The law would be enforced against those who use fraudulent practices to send bulk e-mail, commonly known as spam, to or from Virginia, a state that is headquarters for a number of major Internet providers, including the nation's largest, America Online. 
 The new statute adds criminal penalties for fraudulent, high-volume spammers. It outlaws practices like forging the return address line of an e-mail message or hacking a computer to send spam surreptitiously. Those found guilty of sending more than 10,000 such deceptive e-mail messages in one day would be subject to a prison term of one to five years and forfeiture of profits and assets connected with these activities. 
Public outrage at spam is causing states and Congress to start looking at stronger measures against it. The Internet industry estimates that spam represents nearly half of all e-mail sent. And a new report by the Federal Trade Commission yesterday found that two-thirds of spam is sent with either false return addresses or a misleading subject line. 
Such anger from computer users is even causing some in the industry to support federal legislation, if only to avoid having to deal with a patchwork of state anti-spam laws. More than two dozen states have anti-spam laws, but enforcement problems and low penalties have made many of the laws ineffective.
Virginia's governor, Mark R. Warner, said the new law could have a significant effect on spam because half of all Internet traffic flows through the state. The passage of e-mail through Virginia-based Internet service providers, he said, gives state prosecutors the ability to reach the purveyors of spam in other states and jurisdictions, noting that an earlier, weaker state anti-spam law had survived constitutional challenges.
"Many spammers see the current system that imposes civil fines as just a cost of doing business," Governor Warner said. "We hope we will see some high-profile prosecutions. If someone faces a jail sentence and a major forfeiture of assets, it will serve as a deterrent." 
But some legal experts said they doubted whether Virginia would have as much of an impact as Mr. Warner suggested. Legal cases, they said, would probably be bogged down by questions over jurisdiction. Moreover, there are practical problems that limit the ability of prosecutors to reach beyond their own states. 
Shane Ham, a senior analyst who studies anti-spam legislation for the Progressive Policy Institute, said, "I can't imagine the state attorney general in Virginia getting a lot of cooperation if they call up the police in California saying that they want them to arrest and extradite someone who is wanted for spamming."
About half the states have tried to regulate various aspects of spam. The first bill was passed in Nevada in 1997, and simply required that marketers offer recipients a way to be removed from e-mail lists. Washington state has brought several anti-spam cases based on laws that prohibit sending e-mail to state residents where the sender or the subject line is falsified.
Some states, including California, require unsolicited e-mail to be identified with "ADV" in the subject line. Some of these allow recipients of e-mail that violates these rules to sue the sender and seek monetary damages.
Experts say these state rules do not appear to have had a significant effect on spam but have instead perplexed big corporations, like credit card companies and catalog merchants, that do business by e-mail.
"We need a single strong national policy to deal with spam so that no one can play the states off against each other," Mr. Ham said. Of course, even national legislation would do little to prevent spam sent from overseas, as a great deal is.
Congress, which ignored anti-spam bills introduced in the last four years, is now expected to give serious consideration to several new proposals. Indeed, prospective Congressional action is, in part, a response to anti-spam efforts by the states. 
The Direct Marketing Association, the lobbying group for companies that sell by postal mail and e-mail, has reversed its previous opposition to national anti-spam legislation, largely to secure a more unified set of rules.
The group would particularly like to avoid provisions that could force marketers who are found to violate the law to make payments to individuals who received their e-mail messages.
Earlier this month, Senators Conrad Burns, Republican of Montana, and Ron Wyden, Democrat of Oregon, introduced a bill that would require senders to label unsolicited commercial e-mail and ban them from falsifying the sender's name or the subject. 
This week, Senator Charles E. Schumer of New York introduced a bill, intended, he said, to impose criminal penalties on senders who repeatedly violate the law and to create a national registry of people who do not want to receive spam. "Last year, spam was an annoyance," Mr. Schumer said yesterday. "This year, it is a significant problem, and next year, it could start to really kill the use of e-mail."
Representative Zoe Lofgren, Democrat of California, plans to introduce a bill that, like some state statutes, would require bulk spam to be labeled with "ADV" in the subject line. The bill would also carry out a proposal by Prof. Lawrence Lessig of Stanford forcing the senders of spam to pay computer users a bounty for reporting it.
All these proposals are meant to restrict deceptive marketers -- those selling things like rich-quick schemes and penis enlargers -- as well as merchants of pornography. They do not address the great quantities of nondeceptive marketing e-mail sent by big companies and even the Internet service providers themselves. America Online, for example, sends nearly two million marketing e-mail messages a day to its 35 million members. 
But the diffuse nature of the Internet defies orderly regulation, even at the national level, when e-mail travels from state to state and country to country, often in untraceable ways. Many experts think that fighting spam will require new technological approaches that supplement legislation coming from many sources -- including spam purveyors, Internet access providers and big companies that seek customers by e-mail.
Many anti-spam proposals will be debated in a three-day forum on spam in Washington starting today. The F.T.C. -- which already prosecutes some spammers using existing anti-fraud laws -- is organizing the forum and will take an unusually comprehensive look into the technology and economics of the spammers and the potential techniques to thwart them.
E-mail marketers, like Scott Richter, president of OptInRealBig.com, of Westminster, Colo., will debate with anti-spam activists like Alan Murphy, an investigator for Spamhaus, a British registry meant to identify spammers. (Mr. Richter is prominent on Mr. Murphy's list.)
Even Mr. Richter now says that a national law may be a good idea. "I want one law," he said in an interview. "Now, if someone lives in certain states, I have to put 'ADV' in the header. What if he moves and I don't know it?"
Mr. Richter, however, does not want rules that would restrict his marketing too much, where he sells diet pills, among other things. "I'm a U.S. citizen and I've worked hard to build this business," he said. "I don't want to have to move to the Bahamas or to China."

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST 

 BODY:
HealthSouth Bankruptcy Is Expected This Summer
HealthSouth will probably file for bankruptcy this summer in a prearranged deal with its banks and bondholders, according to the turnaround specialist who is running the company.
 The company has debt of $3.3 billion and missed a payment of about $350 million on a convertible bond issue April 1. Forensic accountants are sifting through 300,000 false bookkeeping entries to determine whether HealthSouth has enough cash flow to cover its debt payments. Page C2. 

DHL Airways to Sell Itself to Chief
DHL Airways, the carrier for DHL Worldwide Express, plans to sell the business to its chairman and chief executive. C5.

Dial Settles Harassment Lawsuit
Dial agreed to pay $10 million to settle a federal lawsuit brought on behalf of women who said the company ignored sexual harassment by men at its plant in Aurora, Ill. The Equal Employment Opportunity Commission said the settlement was its biggest sexual harassment case since a suit against Mitsubishi Motor five years ago. C7.

Global Crossing Sale Under Scrutiny
The government has opened an investigation into the proposed buyout of Global Crossing by companies in Hong Kong and Singapore, people briefed on the inquiry said. C21.

United Attendants Approve Cuts
The flight attendants' union at United Airlines, which represents 19,000 workers, voted to accept wage and benefit cuts worth $314 million a year over the next six years. C2.

Stocks Up on Hope SARS Is Waning
Asian stock markets rose sharply as investors gained hope that an outbreak of severe acute respiratory syndrome, or SARS, could be brought under control. A10.

Vivendi to Sell Some U.S. Divisions
Vivendi Universal plans to sell its American film, television, theme park and video game businesses by the end of the year. C4.

Profits Fall 11% at Bristol-Myers
Bristol-Myers Squibb said earnings fell 11 percent in the quarter on flat sales and increased marketing and promotional expenses. C2.

Ericsson to Eliminate 7,000 Jobs
Carl-Henric Svanberg, left, the new chief executive of L. M. Ericsson, has built a reputation as a cost-cutter at several companies. He plans to eliminate 7,000 jobs at Ericsson and try to make the company small enough to earn a profit. Ericsson lost 4.3 billion Swedish kronor ($520 million) in the first quarter of 2003. World Business, Section W.

Strong Earnings Bolster Markets
Better-than-expected earnings reports sent stocks higher. The Dow Jones industrial average gained 31.38 points, to close at 8,502.99. The Nasdaq composite index increased 9.06 points, to 1,471.30, and the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index advanced 3 points, to 917.84. C9.

Consumer Confidence Rose in April
The Conference Board said its index of consumer confidence in the economy jumped this month by the most since March 1991. C8.

ABB Lost $45 Million in 1st Quarter
ABB reported a loss of $45 million, and attributed the poor showing to the heavy borrowing earlier. World Business, Section W.

Sales and Profits Up at Northrop
Northrop Grumman posted record quarterly sales and higher earnings, and raised its profit estimates for the year. C8.

Improving an Ohio State Neighbor
Ohio State is trying to improve its neighborhood by transforming a 7.4-acre site on High Street into a mixed-use project intended both to attract shoppers and stimulate investment. Commercial Real Estate. C6.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: Former President of NOW Is Taking Helm at Y.W.C.A. 

BYLINE:  By The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 29 

 BODY:
Trying to breathe new life into a 145-year-old organization, the Y.W.C.A. of the U.S.A. has chosen Patricia Ireland, the outspoken former president of the National Organization for Women, to be its new chief executive. 
 Ms. Ireland's appointment, to be announced on Wednesday, coincides with the Y.W.C.A.'s relocation of its national headquarters to Washington from Manhattan. 
 "This is just an irresistible opportunity," Ms. Ireland said in an interview on Monday. "I want to be back in the public policy arena." 
She attributed her selection for the post, which sheis to assume by May 15, to her "unique experience" with NOW and her public visibility.
"The Y.W.C.A. has not had a presence in Washington and not had an emphasis on advocacy at a time that it seems very important," Ms. Ireland said. 
The Y.W.C.A., she said, has programs that include child care, job training and prevention of violence against women, all of which are being hurt by federal budget cuts.
"The local affiliates and local associations have these great programs," she said. "But all the programs are being cut because the budget in Washington is being cut."
At NOW, Ms. Ireland combined grass-roots organizational techniques with an insider's knowledge of Washington to provide the group with a national voice. She vows to do the same in her new position. 
"It's a question of organizing and advocating," she said.Ms. Ireland plans to direct her efforts to gain economic empowerment and racial equality for women and girls, two of the organization's central goals.
"The bottom line is the bottom line," she said. "If we had greater economic empowerment, a lot of other issues would be easier to deal with."
Ms. Ireland said she would work closely with local organizations to determine where Y.W.C.A. advocacy has succeeded or failed. In doing so, she will be able to more effectively lobby for the Y.W.C.A.'s causes on Capitol Hill, she said.
She named possible Y.W.C.A. campaigns: fair voting rights for people of all races (she is a Floridian, and says the 2000 presidential election is still in her mind) and celebrity spokeswomen (she suggested the tennis star Serena Williams as a role model for young women).
"Perhaps we will even have a 'Feed the Models' rally in New York City, in the fashion district," she said, not entirely in jest.
Ms. Ireland also said the Y.W.C.A. headquarters' new site in Washington places it near many resources, including a network of civil rights and social justice groups.
"Part of the joy of this is that I don't have to figure it all out myself," she said.
She is eager, she said, to be a part of the Y.W.C.A.'s "tremendous history," which has included work on behalf of women's suffrage, sex education and racial equality. 
"In one sense, I'm diving back into the women's movement," she said. "The Y.W.C.A. has a certain amount of respectability that I would like to capitalize on."
At NOW, an organization that is pro-women and pro-lesbian and abortion rights, she developed a reputation for bucking conservative norms. A decade ago, Ms. Ireland raised eyebrows by living with another woman in Washington while remaining married to her husband, with whom she recently celebrated their 35th wedding anniversary.
She said she did not know what criteria the Y.W.C.A.'s executive board used in its selection, but she added that "there were certainly no questions about my personal life," in the interview process.
Today, Ms. Ireland, 57, one of America's most prominent feminists, finds herself leading an organization historically associated with traditional Christian values. But she points out that the Y.W.C.A. is "enriched by many faiths" and has an extensive history of progressivism.
"The Y.W.C.A.'s long history includes taking positions that were at the time very radical and distressful to the common culture," she said, noting that in the early 20th century the organization was among the first public advocates of racial integration and equality.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Patricia Ireland says she wants the Y.W.C.A. to have a national voice and a presence in Washington. (Stephen Crowley/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Legislators Pursue a Budget That Reverses Pataki's Cuts 

BYLINE:  By AL BAKER and JAMES C. McKINLEY Jr.

DATELINE: ALBANY, Jan. 29 

 BODY:
Defying Gov. George E. Pataki, state lawmakers began passing a state budget tonight that restores most of the cuts to education and Medicaid that the governor had proposed. The plan would raise both the sales tax and income taxes on the wealthiest New Yorkers to pay for it.
 Legislative leaders also promised to provide more than $1 billion in new revenue for New York City, though they had not agreed on specifics. Along with the restorations of school aid and Medicaid money, the plan would provide Mayor Michael R. Bloomberg with almost all of the assistance he had sought from Albany to help close the city's budget gap, the leaders said. 
 But Mr. Pataki defiantly vowed to veto the Legislature's budget plan and to punish politically any lawmaker who went along with it. 
Still, both chambers immediately started passing appropriation bills for transportation, public protection and some government services by formidable vote tallies. By late evening, lawmakers were preparing for the printer the bill to increase taxes and raise the revenue to pay for their spending.
By moving forward, the Legislature sets the stage for what promises to be a bruising showdown with the governor.
Until midday yesterday, the legislative leaders had hoped that they could get Mr. Pataki to join them in a mutually acceptable budget. The state faces an $11.5 billion budget gap, and the governor has vowed not to raise income taxes to solve the crisis.
In a news conference in his second-floor office this afternoon, Mr. Pataki said that he could not go along with the deal. He said that he would veto any budget bills the Legislature passed and that he would do his best to thwart an override.
"The consequences to the Legislature -- should this plan become law -- are clear," Mr. Pataki said, in a speech that he delivered to reporters and his aides.
"I will do everything in my ability to prevent this plan from becoming law, including exercising my veto powers," the governor said. "I will fight for the people of New York. I will fight for the principles that have made New York a better state. And I will fight to ensure that any legislator, Democrat or Republican, Assembly member or senator, will be held accountable by the people for their choice."
He said the Legislature's plan would require at least $7 billion worth of tax increases over the next two years and would suffocate any chance for an economic recovery in New York, a claim that lawmakers said was inaccurate.
After delivering his statement, the governor pivoted away from a lectern and left the room as his aides applauded loudly, preventing reporters from asking follow-up questions.
Later in the day, details of the budget plan began to trickle out. A legislative aide said the revenue bill includes an increase in the sales tax for the next three years, to 4.25 percent from 4 percent. It also includes a temporary surcharge on those residents who earn an adjusted annual gross income of $150,00 or more. The income tax rate would increase to 7.5 percent from 6.85 percent, then go back down to its current level in three years.
The bill includes many other new revenue sources that could generate as much as $1 billion. 
With momentum seeming to move against him, Mr. Pataki staked his political fortune on the strength of his case. But within minutes of his announcement, in a reflection of the Legislature's determination, the Senate, controlled by the governor's Republican allies, voted 58-to-0 to override Mr. Pataki's veto of a bill that would extend by two weeks the May 20 deadline for school districts around the state to present their budgets to voters. The State Assembly, led by Democrats, passed the bill the previous day by a vote of 111 to 38, with nine Republicans in support.
Legislators from both parties were girding themselves for the likelihood that the three-term governor would make good on his threat to veto their budget, and that they would have to override his veto. For Republicans, it was tantamount to a rebellion against their party leader.
Several Republicans in the State Senate said, however, they would stand solidly with their majority leader, Joseph L. Bruno, despite the governor's threat to oppose them when they face re-election campaigns next year.
Dale M. Volker, a Republican senator from Depew in western New York, said the threat was empty, as he doubted that the governor would truly stand by his vow to hurt fellow Republicans, all of whom are gearing up to play host to President Bush at the presidential nominating convention in New York City in September 2004.
"We are all Republicans," Mr. Volker said. "He is not going to take anybody out."
Senator Volker said the strategies of the governor's political advisers "are old." Much more important to the legislators, he said, was the outcry from their constituents against the governor's budget, which was considered likely to lead to increases in local property taxes to compensate for proposed cuts in school aid, Medicaid and other state assistance.
"This is more than political, much bigger," Mr. Volker said.
A two-thirds majority is needed in each chamber to override the governor's vetoes. In the Assembly, where 103 of the 150 members are Democrats, 101 votes are needed for an override. The governor would need at least four Democrats, assuming all Republicans sided with him.
In the Senate, Mr. Bruno needs four Democratic votes to override the governor's veto, assuming that all 37 of his Republican colleagues stand with him. The chamber has 62 members.
The Assembly Republicans today were the governor's only allies. The 47 Republicans voted as a block against the two budget bills the Assembly passed, contending that they could not support the spending bills until they saw a revenue bill.
That bill began to take shape as the night wore on. In addition to the $2.5 billion in new taxes, and $1 billion in other revenues, the plan would include $4.2 billion worth of borrowing. It can come to a vote in three days.
How the budget battle plays out may depend on the Assembly's minority leader, Charles Nesbitt of Albion.
"We obviously feel very close to the governor and have a lot of respect for his judgment," Mr. Nesbitt said. "It's the state's economy that is providing this money, and it's our responsibility to nurture a healthy state economy."
But when it came to Mr. Pataki's political threat, many lawmakers in both parties shrugged it off. "If the governor looks at his approval ratings, I don't think he's striking fear into anyone's hearts," said Assemblyman Michael N. Gianaris, a Democrat from Queens.
Even Mr. Bruno, Mr. Pataki's ally in Albany, seemed to indicate that lawmakers would rather take their chances with Mr. Pataki than invoke the wrath of property taxpayers. Saying at one point that he considered Mr. Pataki perhaps the greatest governor in state history, he said he nonetheless differed deeply with him in the current budget debate.
"I consider the governor a partner," Mr. Bruno said. "We are having a disagreement at the moment."
He said he considered the move the legislators are making to be courageous. "When this is all said and done, we are going to be representative of the great majority of the people," Mr. Bruno said.
Sheldon Silver, the Assembly speaker, also expressed confidence that the Legislature had reversed what he called the governor's "wrong choices." Asked about the governor's threat to punish lawmakers politically, he said, "I think the governor perhaps should take a step back from his anger and recognize we all have to govern together."
The last time a governor's veto of budget bills was overridden was in 1982, under Hugh L. Carey. Never, however, has a Legislature succeeded in enacting a tax increase that a governor vetoed.
"The real jawbreaker will be the vote to override the veto of a revenue raiser," said William L. Parment, a Democratic Assemblyman from Jamestown. "That's a tough veto to override."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Gov. George E. Pataki said that he would veto any budget bills the Legislature passed and that he would do his best to thwart an override. (Associated Press)(pg. B2)

HEADLINE: World Briefing United Nations: Protesting Cuba's Place On Rights Panel 

BYLINE:  By Daniel B. Schneider (NYT) 

 BODY:
 The United States walked out of a meeting to protest Cuba's election to the United Nations Commission on Human Rights for another three-year term. 
Sichan Siv, the American representative to the United Nations Economic and Social Council, which oversees the rights group, said after the vote: "This is an outrage for us. We view Cuba as the worst violator of human rights in this hemisphere." Regional groups decide who fills seats. Latin America chose six countries, including Cuba, for six open seats, and all six were elected by acclamation. Cuba began its recent crackdown on dissidents as the Human Rights Commission was holding its annual meeting in Geneva. Daniel B. Schneider (NYT)

HEADLINE: As Venezuela Slides, the Poor Stand By Their Man 

BYLINE:  By JUAN FORERO 

DATELINE: CARACAS, Venezuela, April 25 

 BODY:
Unemployed and penniless, Ermis Montilla, 31, lives for now with his family in a shack made of cardboard and corrugated tin. But he has nothing but praise for President Hugo Chavez.
 With building materials from the government, and advice from army engineers, Mr. Montilla is proudly constructing his own home on a hillside of Caracas. He has even become a community leader, imbued with a sense of civic consciousness he says Mr. Chavez has awakened in him. 
 "The opponents of Chavez always promised and promised but never spoke to us," said Mr. Montilla, a father of three. "Now, we have rights. Before, when we talked, no one listened."
But even as President Chavez has cast himself as a champion of the downtrodden, the paradox of his popularity is that there are more and more people like Mr. Montilla: living in poverty but ardent supporters of the president.
Since Mr. Chavez was elected in 1998, poverty has grown an estimated 10 percent and now includes nearly 68 percent of Venezuelans. Economists expect the underclass to grow still more in coming months, as the economy continues a tailspin that began last year in the wake of a short-lived coup against Mr. Chavez and three national strikes led by his opposition.
"If there is more unemployment, more poverty, more crime, why are the poor still with Chavez?" asked Ana Maria Sanjuan, a sociologist at the Central University. "The reason is simple. Chavez is the only one who has addressed the poor, the one who gives a hope to the poor about a possible inclusion."
Beyond his direct apeals to the poor, analysts note, Mr. Chavez has also carefully directed his assistance programs, like the one benefiting Mr. Montilla. 
Taken as a whole, these programs fail to form a broad, coherent public policy, said Luis Pedro Espana, a poverty expert and director of the Institute of Economic and Social Studies at Andres Bello Catholic University in Caracas. But they go a long way toward explaining Mr. Chavez's enduring popularity as the economy shrinks. 
The programs -- ranging from new homes to subsidized food markets to mobile clinics -- are welcomed in long-neglected barrios, deftly spread, and then their importance inflated, Mr. Espana said.
"The government does not really have a social policy," he said. "What they have is social theater."
No one blames Mr. Chavez for bringing poverty -- he won office in 1998 after two decades in which poverty rose to nearly 60 percent from 28 percent of the population.
But the downward economic slide has come hard and fast, particularly since last year. Indeed, the economy may fall by 17 percent, unemployment is approaching 20 percent, thousands of businesses are closing and capital flight amounts to billions of dollars. The government and many economists in the United States blame the effects of a two-month national strike called in December by Chavez opponents. The opposition blames government mismanagement.
In any case, Mr. Chavez retains the support of nearly 40 percent of Venezuelans, higher than other Latin American leaders and vital if he is to avoid losing power in a referendum that opposition groups are demanding for later this year.
Mr. Chavez's poverty programs, and his vocal promotion of the rights of the poor, have been remarkably effective in mobilizing a community as a rule not politically active.
In the wretched neighborhoods ringing Caracas, where the president has drawn most of his support, it is easy to find throngs faithful to the man they call "my commander."
"He moves us and that is why I am here," said Marisela Araujo, 36, as she waited to catch a glimpse of Mr. Chavez at the opening of a government-subsidized food market. "The people here -- we are not paid like the opposition says. No one told us to come. We come here because we love him."
In interviews across several of Caracas's poorest neighborhoods, residents did not readily point to tangible benefits under Mr. Chavez's time in office. Those were seemingly in short supply.
Instead, most quickly pointed to the Constitution, the 50-cent bible of Mr. Chavez's self-styled revolution, for having instilled pride and provided direction.
Critics call the Constitution a mobilizing tool manipulated by Mr. Chavez. But the devoted tout articles in the jumbled document that give rights to people to undertake their own urban planning projects, embark on community activism and allow them to seek titles to plots where they have built makeshift homes.
The Constitution goes hand in hand with government efforts to organize local councils that do everything from paving sidewalks topicking up trash. Even where Venezuelans still live in poverty, they say they now believe they have a voice in the political process.
"The people have learned how to build," said Jose Gregorio Blanco, a community leader. "The president wants people to go and learn, to learn how to get things done."
More tangibly, the government has more than doubled spending on education, mostly for teacher salaries. While other spending is murkier, the government is clearly funneling aid and services toward poor neighborhoods.
In the barrio Las Marias, where Mr. Blanco provided a tour, five buildings with 288 apartments for the poor are being built. In Isaias Medina Angarita, residents thank Mr. Chavez for paved sidewalks. In the Sucre district, the Florencio Jimenez school, long neglected, now serves free lunches and offers expanded school hours for 563 pupils.
The government has also set up people's banks, giving credit to small enterprises. It is handing out titles to poor squatters in Caracas slums. Low-priced or free medical services are provided through roaming clinics. 
The programs have the feel of old-style patronage. With them, Mr. Chavez has managed to erase doubt among followers by taking up the populist script in a way that has not been seen in Latin America in recent years, spending hours with crowds of followers.
In Caricuao, a poor neighborhood of Soviet-style apartment blocks in southwest Caracas, his aides led a hunched group of elderly women from the crowd and provided them seating under a white tent, just feet from the president, who was there to inaugurate the first of what will be 100 markets across the city. Afterward, they expressed everlasting loyalty.
"He does not care if you are rich or poor," explained Irda de Belandria, 66. "His heart is so pure."
But for the neighborhood, the affection for Mr. Chavez may outlast the material effect of the markets, which offer only a modest assortment of goods like canned products, powdered milk and sugar. Even the actual opening was unclear.
The president left, and military officers shut the metal gates over the storefront. One woman asked when it would reopen.
"I do not know," a military official said. "They told us tomorrow, maybe."

HEADLINE: AFTEREFFECTS: NATO; 
4-Nation Plan For Defense Of Europe

BYLINE:  By ELAINE SCIOLINO 

DATELINE: BRUSSELS, April 29 

 BODY:
Their goals may have been laudable, but their timing could not have been worse.
 The leaders of four European nations that opposed the American-led war in Iraq unveiled a plan today for closer cooperation aimed at making Europe's defense more coherent and more independent of that of the United States. 
 In a ballroom of the Hilton Hotel, President Jacques Chirac of France, Chancellor Gerhard Schroder of Germany, Prime Minister Guy Verhofstadt of Belgium and Premier Jean-Claude Juncker of Luxembourg all emphasized -- more than once -- that their security and defense initiative would strengthen, not undermine, the NATO alliance and their relationships with their other European Union partners.
"This is not directed against NATO," Mr. Schroder said. "It's a reinforcement of NATO, because it will strengthen the European pillar." 
But at another point he said, "In NATO we don't suffer from too much America; we suffer from not enough Europe."
Asked about the exclusionary nature of their summit meeting, the four leaders insisted that all they were doing was a bit of creative repackaging, citing proposals, communiques, meetings and initiatives that went back years. "We didn't have a meeting here of putschists, certainly not," Mr. Juncker said.
But perceptions are crucial to diplomacy. And in the face of strong warnings from the United States and Britain not to do anything to harm NATO, the fact that the meeting took place at all sent a strong signal that a core group within NATO and the European Union was prepared to create joint strategies without consulting its partners in advance. 
The summit meeting also left bad feelings among European Union members who were left out.
In particular, the four leaders had been advised not to set up a European military headquarters, on the ground that doing so would risk rivaling the Supreme Headquarters Allied Powers Europe, or Shape, which directs the alliance's military planning from southern Belgium.
But among the group's proposals was the creation of a military center in Belgium in the summer of 2004 for "planning and command" of joint European Union operations outside the NATO theater. Mr. Chirac insisted that the new center would increase efficiency. "This is not about duplicating Shape," he said, "but eliminating duplication by national headquarters."
But in Washington, Secretary of State Colin L. Powell said NATO was more in need of better weapons. "What we need is not more headquarters," he told a Senate Foreign Relations Committee hearing. "What we need is more capability and fleshing out the structure and the forces that are there with the equipment that they need."
Mr. Bush warned France in an interview with NBC News on April 25 not to use its position in Europe "to create alliances against the United States, or Britain or Spain or any of the new countries that are the new democracies in Europe."
Underscoring the centrality of NATO, Mr. Bush said, "It's very important that Europe not become fractured to the point where the United States won't have relations with a united Europe, whole, free and at peace."
Deep suspicions of the four countries remain since their initial refusal of an American request to bolster Turkey's defenses before the war in Iraq, considered the most serious rift in NATO history.
Other initiatives announced at today's meeting include the creation of a joint rapid reaction unit built on an existing French-German brigade into which Belgian commandos and a Luxembourg reconnaissance team will be integrated.
The group also reiterated the need to unite European military air transport, disaster relief, training and arms procurement.
The proposals will be presented to the 11 other members of the European Union this weekend.
There was no mention in the four leaders' communique of increasing Europe's military spending, a sore point within the European Union and in Europe's relationship with Washington.
The European Union's foreign policy chief, Javier Solana, who decided not to attend the summit meeting, said it would be valuable only if it spurred governments to spend more money on their militaries.
France spends 2.6 percent of its gross domestic product on military expenses, but the three other nations at today's meeting are among the lowest military spenders in Europe.
There were calls from politicians and newspapers in Europe to call off today's meeting. In fact, it was seen in the end largely as a maneuver by Mr. Verhofstadt to garner support at home in the weeks before a tight general electionon May 18.

HEADLINE: Union Members Rally at City Hall Against City's Plans for Job Cuts 

BYLINE:  By STEVEN GREENHOUSE 

 BODY:
Thousands of union members rallied outside City Hall last night in the largest protest so far against Mayor Michael R. Bloomberg's planned layoffs and budget cuts.
 Carrying signs that read, "We've just begun to fight," and "Don't balance the budget on our backs," the protesters urged the mayor to cancel the layoffs and instead push for more aid from Albany and for higher taxes on the rich. 
 The rally was organized by District Council 37, the largest municipal union, which has 125,000 members and has been hit harder than any other union by Mr. Bloomberg's plans to lay off 4,500 workers. 
"This fight is not just about D.C. 37 members," said Lillian Roberts, the district council's executive director. "This fight is about the quality of life for all New Yorkers. In order for the city's good years to return, we need a budget that does not work against the city work force."
She was flanked by a handful of politicians and a phalanx of top labor leaders, including Dennis Rivera, president of 1199/S.E.I.U.; Randi Weingarten, president of the United Federation of Teachers; and Roger Toussaint, president of Transport Workers Union Local 100.
The Police Department estimated that there were 15,000 protesters, who stood on either side of Broadway, stretching hundreds of yards north from Park Place to above Chambers Street.
The rally's main message was that Mayor Bloomberg should look anywhere but to layoffs, union concessions or service cuts to trim the city's $4 billion budget. Speaker after speaker said that union members made the city work, that they were not well paid and that it was wrong to squeeze them more to balance the budget.
Mr. Bloomberg has been at loggerheads with the unions over their failure to agree with him on $600 million in savings he asked for on employee benefits.
Struggling to speak over the cheering crowd, Ms. Weingarten, who is also chairwoman of the Municipal Labor Committee, said, "They are saying, 'Either you give up your benefits or you accept layoffs.' Is that a fair choice?"
The crowd thundered, "No!"
At a news conference, Mr. Bloomberg said he was sympathetic to municipal workers. "Nobody wants to lose their job," he said. "Nobody wants to not get raises, and my commitment when I came into office was do the best I can to protect the workers, to downsize only through attrition and through early retirement."
But he said that he had no choice but to order layoffs because some union leaders appeared to find limited layoffs more acceptable than granting concessions, which anger almost all union members.
"They would rather have a handful of happy members left," Mr. Bloomberg said. "I don't know that that's good representation but that's up to the union members to go to their leaders and say what they want to do. If they want to help prevent layoffs they can do it."
The protesters directed their fire at not just the mayor, but also at Gov. George E. Pataki, the rich, and private contractors doing business for the city, insisting that they could all do more to eliminate the city's budget deficit. As for whether the unions had any role in helping close the deficit, the protesters were mum.
"The mayor is out of touch," said Arthur Cheliotes, president of a communications' workers local representing 8,000 city supervisors and middle managers. "He doesn't understand the working people of the city. He and his rich friends continue to get a free ride. The time has come for the rich to pay."
The union members appeared as a sea of green and blue T-shirts, and for many of them, the rally was not so much a time to scream, but a time to relax and chat in the sun with buddies from work.
"I'm here because I don't agree with the layoffs," said Ina Stennett, who earns $25,000 a year after 22 years as a clerical worker in socia services. "Too many people lost their jobs with the World Trade Center, and now we're losing more jobs. I don't see how layoffs are going to help the city."
Even as he said the mayor had a good heart, Yosry Aly, who has worked for 16 yearls as a city tax auditor, faulted Mr. Bloomberg for ordering layoffs in his department, saying they were shortsighted.
"Every tax auditor brings in $1 million to $2 million a year," he said. "If your problem is money, then why are you laying off auditors who bring in money?"
Hundreds of workers from the Civil Service Employees Association, the transport workers and other unions joined the rally. In addition, nearly 1,000 workers from a group that protested the war in Iraq, United for Peace and Justice, marched from Washington Square to join the rally, asserting that less money should go to the Pentagon and more to the nation's cities.
The city's labor unions appear divided over where to focus their criticism. District Council 37 of the American Federation of State, County and Municipal Employees made Mr. Bloomberg its target, while 1199, the giant health care union, held its big demonstration in Albany, to pressure Governor Pataki, and the teachers' union has been planning a rally in Albany to urge the governor to restore his threatened education cuts.
Several of the speakers directed their words at Albany, insisting the governor could do more to bail out the city. Marty Markowitz, the Brooklyn borough president, told the crowd, "Our message is to the governor -- show us the money."
"Our message is to the mayor. You helped elect the governor," he said. "He owes you big time. Now collect your I.O.U.'s."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Union members make the city work, speakers told a like-minded crowd outside City Hall. Mayor Bloomberg has proposed eliminating 4,500 jobs. (Nicole Bengiveno/The New York Times)
